Book of Bequests. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary on al-Hidayah Volume 19. Reducing the bequest (to less than one-third) is an act of maintaining good ties with close relatives by leaving one's wealth to them, as opposed to fulfilling the entire one-third because that is merely receiving one's full right, which is neither maintaining ties nor a favor. Then, is making a bequest of less than one-third more virtuous, or is leaving the bequest (more virtuous)? The mashaa'ikh have said: If the heirs are needy and will not become affluent from the amount of inheritance, then leaving the bequest is more virtuous because in that there is maintaining ties with relatives, and the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said: 'The best charity is upon a relative with whom one has estranged relations,' and because in this both the poor and kinship are considered. Commentary: The Shariah has given a person in his final moments the authority to dispose of one-third of his wealth. Therefore, it is permissible to make a bequest within the one-third, but it is mustahabb for him not to bequeath the entire one-third, whether the heirs are wealthy or poor, but rather to bequeath less. This is because when he bequeaths less, what he leaves for his heirs is an act of kindness and maintaining ties with his relatives, which is a praiseworthy act. If he bequeaths the entire one-third, he has merely received his right, which is neither maintaining ties nor a favor nor kindness. In any case, he should bequeath less than one-third. Then a question arises: Is making a bequest of less than one-third more virtuous, or is leaving it off more virtuous? The fuqaha have given some details. They say: If the heirs are poor and will not become affluent from the inheritance they receive, then in such a case not making a bequest is more virtuous and superior because one gains the reward of maintaining ties with relatives, and in this case both the rights of the poor and of kinship are observed. And in Musnad Ahmad it is narrated: 'The best charity is upon a resentful relative,' i.e., giving charity to one's relative with whom one is not happy is best because it goes against the self. If the heirs are wealthy, is bequeathing better or leaving the bequest? (Arabic text): 'And if they are wealthy or become wealthy through their share, then bequest is better because it becomes charity for a non-relative, and leaving the bequest is a gift for a relative, and the former (charity) is better because one seeks thereby the face of Allah. And it is said: In this case, the testator has a choice because each includes a virtue—charity or maintaining ties—so he is given a choice between two goods.' Translation and commentary: If the heirs are poor and needy, their ruling has passed. If they are wealthy or will become wealthy from the share they receive, then making a bequest is better because it becomes charity, whereas leaving the bequest becomes a gift to a relative; and giving charity to a non-relative is better than giving a gift to a relative because the motive of charity is to seek Allah's pleasure. In this case, some fuqaha have said that the testator has a choice—he may choose charity or gift, because both are good deeds in themselves, so he may choose whichever he wishes.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the preference between making a bequest of less than one-third and leaving no bequest, dependent on the financial status of the heirs. The author begins by stating that reducing one's bequest to less than one-third of the estate is an act of maintaining good ties (silah rahmi) with close relatives, because by leaving more wealth to them, the testator does them a favor. In contrast, bequeathing the full one-third is merely taking one's legal right from the estate, which is neither a favor nor an act of maintaining ties. Therefore, it is mustahabb (recommended) to bequeath less than one-third, whether the heirs are rich or poor. The question then arises: Is it more virtuous to bequeath less than one-third, or to leave no bequest at all? The mashaa'ikh (the Hanafi jurists) have detailed the answer: If the heirs are needy and the inheritance they receive will not make them self-sufficient, then leaving no bequest is more virtuous. This is because by not bequeathing, the testator leaves more for his relatives, which is an act of maintaining ties—a highly recommended deed. The Prophet ﷺ is reported to have said, 'The best charity is upon a resentful relative,' meaning it is especially virtuous to give to a relative with whom one has a strained relationship because it involves self-effacement. Moreover, leaving the wealth to needy relatives serves both the poor and kinship. If the heirs are wealthy, or will become wealthy from their share, then making a bequest to a non-relative is better. This is because the bequest becomes charity (sadaqah), and seeking Allah's face through charity is more virtuous than giving a gift (hibah) to a relative. Some scholars hold that in this case the testator is given a choice between these two virtuous acts: either he may give charity to a non-relative by bequeathing, or he may give a gift to his relative by leaving no bequest. Both are good, but charity is generally preferred because its intent is purely for Allah.

Key Terms
صلہ رحمی (ṣilah raḥmiyyah): Maintaining good ties and kindness to blood relatives, especially by giving them financial benefit.
وصیت (waṣiyyah): A bequest or will made by the testator regarding distribution of up to one-third of his estate to non-heirs.
ثلث (thuluth): One-third of the testator's estate, the maximum amount he can bequeath freely without heirs' permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi jurists in this passage, when is it more virtuous to leave no bequest at all?
A) When the testator has no close relatives.
B) When the heirs are wealthy and do not need the inheritance.
C) When the heirs are poor and will not become self-sufficient from the inheritance.
D) When the testator wants to give charity to non-relatives.

Key Takeaway
The virtue of bequest or its omission depends on the heirs' financial need: if they are poor, leaving no bequest is better; if they are wealthy, bequeathing to non-relatives is better.

Bequest Ownership Requires Acceptance
Explaining Hanafi position that bequest ownership requires acceptance, contra Zufar and al-Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires acceptance for bequest ownership and distinguish it from inheritance using practical proofs.

Core Idea
Bequest creates new ownership; acceptance is required—unlike inheritance which is automatic.

This passage addresses a key difference between bequest and inheritance in Hanafi fiqh. While inheritance transfers ownership immediately without consent, a bequest requires the legatee's acceptance to take effect. The Hanafi scholars present a rational proof based on the nature of ownership and contractual rights.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Summarize the ruling from Al-Quduri: the bequeathed property is owned (yumlak) by acceptance (bil-qabul).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that Imam Zufar (within Hanafi) and one opinion of al-Shafi'i hold that ownership transfers automatically without acceptance.
The opponent argues using analogy (qiyas) with inheritance.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their proof: bequest is 'sister of inheritance' because both are forms of khilafah (vicegerency). Since inheritance does not require acceptance, bequest should not either.
Khilafah means the beneficiary steps into the deceased's place.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: bequest creates new ownership (milk jadid), while inheritance is a continuation. The practical difference is that the legatee cannot return a defective item to the seller, nor can a buyer return a sold item to the legatee.
These rights exist in inheritance but not in bequest.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, if the legatee does not accept, the property remains with the estate (heirs). Under the opposing view, the legatee becomes owner immediately even without acceptance.
This affects who bears the risk and who can dispose of the property.

تشریح اس مسئلہ کی کچھ تفصیل ابھی پہلے گزر چکی ہے جہاں فرمایا گیا تھا کہ قبول وصیت بعد الموت معتبر ہے۔ بہر حال ہمارے نزدیک جب تک موضی از قبول نہ کرے تب تک وہ موضی یہ کا مالک نہ ہو گا اس میں امام زفر اور ایک قول کے مطابق امام شافعی کا اختلاف ہے یہ حضرات فرماتے ہیں کہ بغیر قبول کے موصی لیہ موضی یہ کا مالک ہو جائے گا۔ ان حضرات کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وصیت میراث کی بہن ہے اور میراث میں بغیر قبول وارث کے ملکیت ثابت ہو جاتی ہے اسی طرح بغیر قبول کے وصیت کے اندر بھی ملکیت ثابت ہوگی اس لئے کہ میراث اور وصیت دونوں کے اندرمیت کی طرف سے خلافت ہے کیونکہ دونوں میں مورث کی طرف سے ملکیت منتقل ہو کر وارث یا موضعی لہ کی طرف آتی ہے۔

He (al-Quduri) said: The bequeathed property is owned by acceptance, contrary to Zufar and one of the two opinions of al-Shafi'i. He says: Bequest is the sister of inheritance, because each is a vicegerency (khilafah) since it is a transfer. Then inheritance is established without acceptance, so too bequest. Our proof is that bequest is the creation of new ownership; therefore the legatee cannot return it due to a defect, nor can it be returned to him due to a defect, and one cannot create ownership for another except by his acceptance. As for inheritance, these rulings are established compulsorily by Shariah without acceptance. (Urdu translation and commentary.) Commentary: Some detail of this issue has passed earlier where it was stated that acceptance after death is valid. In any case, according to us, until the legatee accepts, he does not own the bequeathed property. Imam Zufar and, according to one opinion, Imam al-Shafi'i differ. They say that without acceptance the legatee becomes owner. Their proof is that bequest is sister to inheritance, and in inheritance ownership is established without acceptance, so likewise in bequest. This is because both bequest and inheritance are vicegerency from the deceased, since ownership transfers from the testator to the legatee or heir. Our proof is that inheritance is vicegerency; it does not involve creation of new ownership. But bequest involves creation of new ownership. The clear proof of this is that if a testator purchases something and bequeaths it, e.g., to Zaid, then Zaid finds it defective, he does not have the right to return it to the seller due to this defect. Also, if a sick person bequeaths his entire estate, e.g., to Zaid, then the sick person sells something from the estate, and the buyer finds a defect in it, the buyer does not have the right to return it to the legatee. If ownership in bequest were established by vicegerency, both of these rights would exist.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding when ownership of a bequeathed property transfers to the legatee. The Hanafi position, stated by Imam al-Quduri, is that the legatee only owns the property upon his acceptance (al-qabul) after the testator's death. This is opposed by Imam Zufar (from the Hanafi school) and one opinion of Imam al-Shafi'i, who argue that ownership transfers automatically without acceptance, just as inheritance passes without acceptance. Their reasoning is that both bequest and inheritance are forms of khilafah (vicegerency): the property transfers from the deceased to the beneficiary. The Hanafi response distinguishes between inheritance and bequest. Inheritance is a continuation of the deceased's ownership, where the heir steps into the deceased's shoes regarding the estate—no new ownership is created; it is simply a transfer of existing ownership. Bequest, on the other hand, creates new ownership (milk jadid). The testator could have used the property during his life, and the bequest is an act of bestowing new ownership after death. Therefore, acceptance is necessary. The Hanafi proof is practical: if a testator purchases an item and bequeaths it, and the legatee finds a defect, he cannot return it to the seller because he did not have a contractual relationship with the seller. Similarly, if the testator sells an item from the bequeathed estate before death, and the buyer finds a defect, the buyer cannot return it to the legatee because the legatee only owns it after acceptance. If bequest were like inheritance, the legatee would have the right of return in these cases, but he does not, proving that ownership is new and requires acceptance.

Key Terms
موصى به (mūṣā bihī): The property or thing that is bequeathed in a will.
قبول (qabūl): The voluntary acceptance of the bequest by the legatee after the testator's death, which is a necessary condition for ownership to transfer.
خلافت (khilāfah): Vicegerency or succession; the idea that the heir or legatee steps into the deceased's position regarding ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why does the legatee not have the right to return a defective bequeathed item to the seller?
A) Because the legatee owns the item automatically without contract.
B) Because the legatee's ownership is new and not a continuation of the testator's contract.
C) Because the seller is not responsible for defects after death.
D) Because the bequest is invalid if the item is defective.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires the legatee's acceptance for ownership of a bequest because bequest creates new ownership, unlike inheritance which is a continuation.

Wasiyyah — Bequest

Ownership of Bequeathed Property by Acceptance
Inter-madhhab dispute on requirement of acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the legatee owns the bequeathed property only upon acceptance and evaluate the opposing proof of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A bequest creates new ownership, not mere succession; acceptance is essential for ownership.

The passage addresses a fundamental question in Islamic inheritance and bequest law: does a legatee automatically own the bequeathed property upon the testator's death, or is acceptance required? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, insists on acceptance, distinguishing bequest from inheritance. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the dispute with Imam Zufar and one opinion of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the text: the legatee owns the bequeathed property only upon acceptance (qabūl) after the testator's death.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Zufar and one Shafi'i opinion hold that acceptance is not needed, analogizing bequest to inheritance (sister of inheritance). Understand their dalil.
Their proof is qiyas: inheritance establishes ownership without acceptance, so should bequest.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response distinguishes bequest as new ownership (mulk jadīd) versus inheritance as mere succession (khilāfa). The key evidence: the right of return for defects applies in inheritance but not in bequest.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on practical legal effects.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the legatee dies before accepting, according to the opponent the bequest becomes part of the legatee's estate; according to Hanafis, it does not (except istihsan).
This affects estate distribution and inheritance rights of the legatee's heirs.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a case, determine ownership status: e.g., testator dies leaving a bequest to Zayd. Zayd dies before accepting. Under Hanafi, Zayd never owned the property; it returns to testator's estate. Under opponent, it would belong to Zayd's heirs.
The difference is critical for estate planning and litigation.

He said: The bequeathed property is owned by acceptance, except Zufar, and it is one of the two sayings of al-Shafi'i. He says: The will is the sister of inheritance, because each is a khilāfa (succession) due to it being a transfer. Then inheritance is established without acceptance, so likewise the will. And for us: The will is an establishment of new ownership; therefore the legatee cannot return it for a defect, nor can it be returned to him for a defect, and he cannot establish ownership for another without his acceptance. As for inheritance, these rulings are established by force of law (jabran min al-shar‘) without acceptance, because it is a khilāfa. Translation: Quduri said: The bequeathed property comes into ownership by acceptance, contrary to Zufar, and this is one of the two sayings of al-Shafi'i. He says: The will is the sister of inheritance, because each is a khilāfa, since it is a transfer. Then inheritance is established without acceptance, so likewise the will. Our proof is that the will is an establishment of new ownership; for this reason it cannot be returned by the legatee for a defect, nor can it be returned to him for a defect, and he does not have the power to establish ownership for another without his acceptance. In any case, inheritance is a khilāfa, such that these rulings apply in inheritance, and ownership is established by force of law without acceptance. Commentary: Some detail of this issue has passed earlier where it was said that acceptance after death is valid. In any case, according to us, until the legatee accepts, he does not own the bequeathed property. Imam Zufar and according to one saying of Imam al-Shafi'i differ. They say that the legatee becomes owner without acceptance. Their proof is that the will is the sister of inheritance, and in inheritance ownership is established without acceptance; similarly in the will without acceptance. This is because both inheritance and will involve khilāfa from the deceased, as ownership transfers from the testator to the heir or legatee. Our proof is that inheritance is khilāfa; it does not involve establishment of new ownership. The will involves establishment of new ownership. In inheritance, the heir takes the place of the testator without establishment of new ownership. The clear proof that the will involves new ownership is that if the testator bought an item and bequeathed it to, say, Zayd, and Zayd finds a defect in it, Zayd does not have the option to return it to the testator's seller. And if a sick person bequeaths all his wealth to, say, Zayd, then the sick person sells something from the estate, and the buyer finds a defect, the buyer does not have the right to return it to the legatee. If the legatee's ownership were by way of khilāfa, he would have that right in both cases. Summary: The will involves establishment of new ownership, and no one can establish ownership for another without that person's acceptance. As for inheritance, it is by way of khilāfa; therefore the rulings of return (radd) apply in inheritance: the heir has the right to return a defective item purchased by the testator to the seller, and the buyer has the right to return a defective item purchased from the testator to the heir. Whatever is established by way of khilāfa does not depend on acceptance but is established by force of law without acceptance.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage discusses when a legatee (mūṣā lahu) becomes owner of the bequeathed property (mūṣā bihi). According to the Hanafi school (Imam Abu Hanifa and the majority), ownership is established only upon the legatee's acceptance (qabūl) after the testator's death. Imam Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, and one opinion of Imam al-Shafi'i hold that ownership is established without acceptance, similar to inheritance. The Hanafi proof is that a bequest creates new ownership (mulk jadīd), not simply a transfer of ownership as in inheritance. Evidence: if the bequeathed item is defective, the legatee cannot return it to the seller (because he did not inherit the right of return; he receives new ownership). Similarly, if the testator sold part of the estate, the buyer cannot return the defective item to the legatee. In inheritance, the heir steps into the testator's shoes (khilāfa) and thus has the right of return. The passage clarifies the distinction: inheritance is forced by law (jabran min al-shar‘) without acceptance, while a bequest requires the legatee's consent to receive the new ownership. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
موصى به (mūṣā bihi): The item or property that is bequeathed in a will.
موصى له (mūṣā lahu): The person (legatee) to whom the bequest is made.
خلافة (khilāfa): Succession; the heir steps into the testator's position (in inheritance) without new ownership, inheriting all rights and duties.
ملک جدید (mulk jadīd): New ownership; ownership that begins with the legatee, not a continuation of the testator's ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the key distinction between bequest (wasiyyah) and inheritance (irth) regarding ownership?
A) Bequest is voluntary, inheritance is compulsory.
B) Bequest creates new ownership (mulk jadīd), inheritance is succession (khilāfa).
C) Both require acceptance.
D) Inheritance requires court approval.

Key Takeaway
A bequest does not transfer ownership automatically; the legatee must accept, because it creates new ownership, unlike inheritance which is mere succession.

Wasiyyah — Bequest
intermediate

Legatee Dies Before Accepting: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Ruling on bequest when legatee dies before acceptance: istihsan vs qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the legatee dies before acceptance and explain why istihsan overrides the apparent qiyas.

Core Idea
A bequest is complete from the testator's side; the legatee's death before acceptance does not void it — by istihsan.

In this lesson, we examine a specific exception to the rule that a bequest requires acceptance. When the testator dies and then the legatee dies before acceptance, the apparent qiyas would invalidate the bequest. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to preserve the bequest in favor of the legatee's heirs. We will trace the reasoning and the analogy to a sale with an option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and the exception
The general rule is that ownership in a bequest is suspended upon the legatee's acceptance. The exception: if the testator dies and then the legatee dies before acceptance, the bequest passes to the legatee's heirs by istihsan.
2
Examine the qiyas argument
What is the analogy that would invalidate the bequest? It compares the legatee's position to a buyer who dies after the seller's offer but before acceptance; the sale is void. Similarly, the bequest would be void because acceptance never occurred.
This is the apparent qiyas.
3
Analyze the istihsan reasoning
Why does the Hanafi school prefer istihsan? Because the testator's part is complete (tāmm) upon his death; it cannot be rescinded by him. The bequest is suspended only for the legatee's benefit, so if he dies, the bequest is deemed to have been accepted in his favor.
The testator's side is irrevocable.
4
Compare to a sale with khiyar
The text draws an analogy to a sale with an option (khiyar) for the buyer. If the buyer dies before exercising the option, the sale is finalized in his favor. Similarly, the legatee's death finalizes the bequest for his heirs.
The khiyar analogy supports istihsan.
5
State the practical ruling
Thus, by istihsan, the bequeathed property enters the ownership of the legatee's heirs upon his death, even without explicit acceptance.
This is the mufta bihi position.

He said: Except in one issue: that the testator dies, then the legatee dies before acceptance, so the bequeathed property enters into the ownership of his (the legatee's) heirs by istihsan. And by qiyas, the bequest would be invalid, because as we stated, ownership is suspended upon acceptance; it becomes like the death of the buyer before his acceptance after the seller's offer. The reason for istihsan is that the bequest on the part of the testator has become complete (tāmm) by his death; it cannot be rescinded by him. It was only suspended for the right of the legatee. So when the legatee dies, it enters into his ownership, just as in a sale where a condition of option (khiyar) is stipulated for the buyer, if he dies before acceptance. Translation: Quduri said: Except in one issue: that the testator dies, then before acceptance the legatee dies, then by istihsan the bequeathed property enters the ownership of the legatee's heirs. By qiyas, the bequest would be invalid because ownership is suspended on acceptance; it is like the buyer's death after the seller's offer but before his acceptance. The reason for istihsan is that the bequest on the testator's part is completed by his death; it cannot be rescinded by him. The bequest was only suspended for the legatee's right. So when the legatee dies, it enters his ownership, just as in a sale where a condition of option is stipulated for the buyer, if he dies before exercise of the option. Commentary: The bequeathed property enters the legatee's ownership when acceptance occurs, but this one issue is an exception by istihsan. That is: first the testator dies, and before the legatee accepts, he also dies. Here, by istihsan, it is ruled that the legatee's heirs inherit the bequeathed property. Although by qiyas the bequest should be invalid because acceptance was not received; it would be like the buyer's death before acceptance after the offer, so the offer becomes void. Similarly, here the bequest should be void, but we leave qiyas and act on istihsan. The proof of istihsan is that the bequest from the testator is complete (tāmm); therefore, it cannot be rescinded. The bequest is only suspended due to the legatee's right; he has the choice to reject or accept. But if he dies before acceptance, the bequeathed property automatically enters his ownership. The analogy is a sale with a condition of option (khiyar) for the buyer, and the buyer dies before exercising the option within three days.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a specific exception to the general rule that the legatee must accept the bequest. The general rule is that ownership is suspended upon acceptance. However, if the testator dies and then the legatee dies before accepting, the bequeathed property passes to the legatee's heirs by istihsan (juristic preference), not by qiyas (analogy). The qiyas would invalidate the bequest because the required acceptance never occurred, like a buyer dying before accepting an offer. The istihsan reasoning is that the testator's part of the bequest is complete (tāmm) upon his death; it is irrevocable from his side. The bequest is suspended only for the legatee's benefit, giving him the option to accept or reject. If he dies before exercising that option, the bequest is deemed to have been accepted in his favor, so his heirs inherit it. This is analogous to a sale with a khiyar (option) condition: if the buyer dies before exercising the option, the sale is finalized in his favor (i.e., his heirs may take it). This ruling is from the Hanafi school (Imam Abu Hanifa and the majority). The authority weight: it is a position from the Zahir al-Riwayah, as it appears in Quduri's Mukhtasar and the commentary.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; setting aside the apparent qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling based on a stronger proof.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here, the apparent analogy would make the bequest invalid due to lack of acceptance.
خیار (khiyar): Option; right to cancel or confirm a contract. Here, the analogy compares the legatee's right of acceptance to a buyer's option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the testator dies and then the legatee dies before accepting the bequest, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The bequest is void because acceptance was never given.
B) The bequeathed property goes to the testator's heirs.
C) The bequeathed property goes to the legatee's heirs by istihsan.
D) The bequest is postponed until the legatee's heirs decide.

Key Takeaway
By istihsan, a bequest is not voided by the legatee's death before acceptance; it passes to his heirs.

Legatee Dies Before Acceptance: Istihsan Exception
Ruling on bequest entering legatee's estate if he dies before acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a legatee dies before acceptance and explain the legal basis of istihsan over qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a bequest is perfected at the testator's death, so the legatee's death before acceptance transfers the property to his heirs by istihsan.

This lesson examines a specific exception in the law of bequests. While the general principle is that acceptance is necessary for ownership, the Hanafi school applies istihsan when the legatee dies before accepting after the testator's death. Understanding this ruling reveals how istihsan operates as a source of law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the legatee's death before acceptance after the testator's death. The ruling is that the bequeathed item enters the legatee's estate by istihsan.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is istihsan, which is based on the irrevocability of the bequest from the testator's side upon his death.
Istihsan overrides the strict qiyas.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The bequest becomes complete (tamm) from the testator's side at his death; he cannot revoke. The suspension for acceptance is only for the legatee's benefit. Therefore, when the legatee dies, the right is perfected and the property passes to his heirs.
This distinguishes the case from a sale.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The opposing qiyas would void the bequest, analogizing it to a sale where the buyer dies before acceptance.
This is the view attributed to al-Shafi'i.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi school adopts istihsan because the bequest is different from a sale—the testator cannot revoke once he has died, making the legatee's right stronger.
Al-Marghinani endorses this position in al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الافى مسألة واحدة وهو ان يموت الموصى ثم يموت الموصى له قبل القبول فيدخل الموصى به في ملک ورثته استحسانا والقياس ان تبطل الوصية لما بينا ان الملك موقوف على القبول فصار كموت المشترى قبل قبوله بعد ايجاب البائع وجه الاستحسان ان الوصية من جانب الموصى قد تمت بموته تماما لا يلحقه الفسخ من جهته وانما توقفت لحق الموصى له فاذا مات دخل في ملكه كما في البيع المشروط فيه الخيار للمشترى اذا مات قبل الاجازة
He (al-Quduri) said: In one case, it is that the testator dies, then the legatee dies before acceptance, so the bequeathed item enters the ownership of the legatee's heirs by istihsan. The analogy is that the bequest becomes invalid because, as we explained, ownership is suspended upon acceptance. So it becomes like the death of the buyer before acceptance after the seller's offer. The basis of istihsan is that the bequest from the testator's side is completed by his death, irrevocably from his side, and it was only suspended for the sake of the legatee's right. So when he dies, it enters his ownership, like in a sale with a condition of option for the buyer when he dies before ratification.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam al-Quduri's matn regarding a specific case in bequest law. The general principle is that ownership of a bequeathed item depends on the legatee's acceptance; without acceptance, ownership does not transfer. However, by istihsan, if the testator dies and then the legatee dies before accepting, the bequeathed item passes to the legatee's heirs. Qiyas would hold the bequest void, as the legatee never accepted. The istihsan is based on the fact that the bequest becomes fully effective from the testator's side upon his death—he cannot revoke it—and the suspension is merely to protect the legatee's right of choice. Therefore, when the legatee dies, the right is perfected, and the property enters his estate automatically. The analogy is drawn to a conditional sale where the buyer has an option; if the buyer dies before exercising it, the sale is finalized and the property goes to his heirs.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A legal method that abandons strict qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling based on a stronger textual indication or custom.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning that would void the bequest by analogy to a sale where the buyer dies before acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where the testator dies and then the legatee dies before acceptance, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The bequest becomes void because acceptance is essential.
B) The bequeathed item passes to the legatee's heirs by istihsan.
C) The bequest is suspended until a new legatee is appointed.
D) The property returns to the testator's heirs.

Key Takeaway
By istihsan, a bequest is not voided by the legatee's death before acceptance; it transfers to his heirs.

Bequest Void When Debts Encompass Entire Wealth
Ruling on bequest by a debtor whose debts cover all assets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a bequest made by a debtor whose debts cover all his wealth, and identify the exception when creditors forgive the debt.

Core Idea
Debt repayment is obligatory; bequests are voluntary – obligations always come first.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: a person wishes to make a bequest but is burdened with debts. Islamic law prioritizes debt repayment over voluntary dispositions. We will examine the ruling from the Hanafi school, the rationale behind it, and the exceptional case where creditors discharge the testator, allowing the bequest to take effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The act is a bequest (wasiyyah), which is a voluntary disposition (tabarru'). Recognize that it is not obligatory.
2
Identify the condition triggering the ruling
The condition is that the testator's debts (dayn) encompass his entire wealth. No surplus remains after debt repayment.
The Arabic phrase is 'دين يحيط بماله' (debt that encircles his wealth).
3
State the hukm (legal ruling)
The bequest is not valid (la tajuz). It is effectively prohibited because it conflicts with the prior obligation of debt repayment.
The hukm level is haram (invalid) in the sense that it is not permissible to execute such a bequest.
4
Understand the rationale
Debt repayment is obligatory (fard), while the bequest is voluntary (tabarru'). The more important need takes precedence. Islamic law always begins with the most important.
This is based on the principle 'الاهم فالأهم' (the more important first).
5
Identify the exception
If the creditors discharge the testator from the debt (i.e., forgive it), the bequest becomes effective because the obstacle is removed.
The exception is mentioned in the text: 'الا ان تبرئه الغرماء'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن أوصى وعليه دين يحيط بماله لم تجز الوصية لان الدين مقدم على الوصية لانه اهم الحاجتين فانه فرض والوصية تبرع وابدا يبدأ بالاهم الا ان تبرئه الغرماء لانه لم يبق الدين فتنفذ الوصية على الحد المشروع لحاجته اليها
He said: Whoever makes a bequest while he has a debt that encircles his wealth, the bequest is not valid, because debt takes precedence over the bequest. This is because it (debt repayment) is the more important of the two needs, for it (debt) is an obligation, while the bequest is a voluntary donation. And always begins with the most important. Except if the creditors discharge him from the debt, because then no debt remains, so the bequest becomes effective to the extent prescribed, due to his need for it.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This is a clear ruling from Imam al-Quduri's matn: a person whose debts cover all his assets cannot make a valid bequest. The reason is that debt repayment is obligatory (fard), while a bequest is a voluntary act (tabarru'). In Islamic law, obligations are prioritized over optional acts. Therefore, debts must be paid first from the estate before any bequest can take effect. However, if the creditors forgive the debt, the bequest becomes valid because the obstacle is removed. The 'need' refers to the testator's desire to allocate his wealth for charitable or other purposes, which is legitimate only after debts are settled.

Key Terms
دين (dayn): A debt owed by the testator that must be paid from the estate before any bequests.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary donation, grant, or gift, including bequests, as opposed to obligatory payments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a bequest invalid when the testator's debts cover his entire wealth?
A) Because the bequest would exceed the one-third limit.
B) Because debt repayment is obligatory and takes precedence over voluntary bequests.
C) Because the testator is considered bankrupt.
D) Because the creditors must approve the bequest.

Key Takeaway
A bequest is invalid if the testator's debts cover all assets; only after debts are settled can a bequest take effect.

Minor's Bequest: Hanafi Rejection vs Shafi'i Acceptance
Dispute on validity of bequest by a minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a minor's bequest, identify the Shafi'i counter-argument, and explain the Hanafi response based on legal capacity.

Core Idea
A minor lacks full legal capacity, so any bequest is invalid regardless of charitable intent.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute: can a minor (sabi) make a valid bequest? The Hanafi school firmly rejects it, while the Shafi'i school allows it for charitable purposes. We will explore the proofs on both sides and understand why the Hanafis hold that legal capacity (ahliyyah) is a prerequisite for any disposition of property.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the explicit ruling in the text: 'la tasihh wasiyyat al-sabi' – the bequest of a minor is not valid.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a minor's bequest is valid if it is for charitable purposes (wujuh al-khayr).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Two proofs: (1) The precedent of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA) who permitted the bequest of a yafi' (boy near puberty). (2) It benefits the minor by earning reward, and if not executed, the property goes to heirs anyway.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is based on the principle that a minor lacks full legal capacity (ahliyyat al-ada') for any disposition. 'Umar's action is considered a specific case, not a general binding precedent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: A Hanafi judge would reject a minor's bequest; a Shafi'i judge may uphold it for charitable purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا تصح وصية الصبى وقال الشافعى تصح اذا كان فى وجوه الخير لان عمر رضى الله عنه اجازوصية خ او يافع وهو الذى راهق الحلم ولانه نظر له بصرفه الى نفسه فى نيل الزلفى ولو لم تنفذ يبقى على غيره
He said: The bequest of a minor is not valid. Al-Shafi'i said: It is valid if it is for charitable purposes, because 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) permitted the bequest of a khal or a yafi' (a boy approaching puberty). And because it is a consideration for him (the minor) by spending it on himself to attain nearness (to Allah). And if it is not executed, it remains for others (i.e., his heirs).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position that a minor's bequest is invalid, and the Shafi'i view that it is valid when directed to charitable channels. The Hanafi school requires the testator to be of sound mind and mature (baligh) for a bequest to be valid. Imam Shafi'i argues based on two proofs: first, the practice of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA) who validated the bequest of a boy who had reached the age of discernment (yafi', near puberty); second, it benefits the minor by allowing him to gain divine reward, and if not executed, the wealth would go to his heirs anyway (who may not spend it charitably). The Hanafi response, implied in the text, is that the bequest of a minor is not legally effective because the minor lacks full legal capacity (ahliyyat al-ada'). The reference to 'Umar's action is considered a specific case, not a general rule, and the Hanafi school does not accept it as binding precedent since it contradicts the principle of capacity.

Key Terms
صبى (sabi): A minor child who has not reached puberty and lacks full legal capacity for property dispositions.
يافع (yafi'): A boy approaching puberty (muhtalim), roughly age 12-15, considered by some to have partial capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a minor's bequest invalid?
A) Because minors cannot own property.
B) Because a minor lacks full legal capacity to execute a bequest.
C) Because 'Umar (RA) prohibited it.
D) Because the bequest would exceed one-third.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects a minor's bequest due to lack of full legal capacity, even for charitable purposes, diverging from the Shafi'i view.

Will Invalid When Debt Encompasses All Wealth
States ruling and rationale for invalid will when debt covers all wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a will is invalid when debt covers all assets, and explain the reasoning using the priority of fard over tabarru'.

Core Idea
Debt repayment is fard; will is tabarru'. When debt covers all wealth, the will is void.

This lesson addresses a critical rule in Islamic inheritance: the priority of debts over bequests. In the Hanafi school, a will is a voluntary act (tabarru'), while repaying debts is an obligation (fard). When a person's debts consume their entire estate, any will they make is invalid because the creditors' rights come first. However, if creditors forgive the debt, the will can become valid. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for both testators and legal practitioners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the passage to determine the specific ruling: making a will when debt encompasses all wealth is not valid (la tajuz). This is the core legal conclusion.
The passage is from al-Quduri's matn, a foundational text of the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the rationale
The passage explains the reasoning: debt repayment is fard (obligatory), while a will is tabarru' (voluntary). In conflict, obligations take precedence over voluntary acts. This is a consistent principle in Islamic law.
The principle is 'al-ahamm fa al-ahamm' (the more important first).
3
Note the exception
If the creditors forgive the debt, the will becomes valid because the obstacle (debt) is removed. The testator's need for a will (e.g., to benefit non-heirs) is considered.
The exception is based on the removal of the preventing cause.
4
Review additional clarifications
The passage adds two tanbih (notes): (1) For a person with no heirs and no debt, it is preferable to give charity directly and then bequeath the rest. (2) A musta'min (non-Muslim under protection) can bequeath all his property because he has no Muslim heirs.
These clarifications show the scope and limits of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوصايا
۱۰۸
اشرف الهدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۶
اندر اندر ہی وہ مر گیا تو بیع نام ہو جاتی ہے اور مبیع مشتری کے ورثا کے لئے ہوتی ہے ایسے ہی یہاں بھی ہے۔ ایک شخص جتنے مال کا مالک ہے اتنے ہی کا مقروض بھی ہے اس کی لئے وصیت کرنا ناجائز ہے
قال ومن أوصى وعليه دين يحيط بماله لم تجز الوصية لان الدين مقدم على الوصية لانه اهم الحاجتين فانه فرض والوصية تبرع وابدا يبدأ بالاهم الا ان تبرئه الغرماء لانه لم يبق الدين فتنفذ الوصية على الحد المشروع لحاجته اليها
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس نے وصیت کی اور اس پر اتنا قرض ہے جو اس کے مال کو محیط ہے تو وصیت جائز نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ قرض وصیت پر مقدم ہے اس لئے کہ قرض دونوں حاجتوں میں زیادہ اہم ہے اس لئے کہ اداء قرض فرض اور وصیت تبرع ہے اور ہمیشہ اہم سے ابتداء کی جاتی ہے پھر اس سے جو اس کے بعد اہم ہو مگر یہ کہ قرض خواہ اس کو بری کر دیں اس لئے کہ قرض باقی نہیں رہا تو وصیت اس حد تک نافذ ہو جائے گی جو مشروع ہے وصیت کی جانب موصی کی حاجت کی وجہ سے۔
تشریح اگر کسی شخص پر اتنا قرض ہو جو اس کے مال کو محیط ہے تو اس کیلئے وصیت کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کہ دین وصیت پر مقدم ہے اوائل سراجی میں اس کی بحث گزر چکی ہے، پھر دین اور وصیت میں زیادہ اہم حاجت اداء دین ہے کیونکہ اداء دین فرض ہے اور وصیت تو محض ایک تبرع ہے اور ہمیشہ کا مسلم اصول ہے تو پہلے اہم سے ابتدا ہوتی ہے پھر درجہ بدرجہ اور جب اہم قرض ہے تو ابتدا اسی سے ہوگی اس کے بعد وصیت کا نمبر ہے، البتہ اگر قرض خواہوں نے مقروض کو قرض سے بری کر دیا ہو تو اب اس کی وصیت مذکورہ جائز ہو جائے گی کیونکہ قرض ختم ہونے کی وجہ سے مانع زائل ہو چکا ہے لہذا جس صورت میں جتنی وصیت جائز ہے وہ اب سب جائز ہو جائیں گی کیونکہ موسی وصیت کا محتاج ہے جس کی وجہ کتاب الوصایا کے شروع میں گزر چکی ہے۔
تنبیہا جس کا نہ کوئی وارث ہو اور نہ اس پر قرض ہو تو اس کے لئے افضل یہ ہے کہ اپنے ہاتھ سے صدقہ کرنے کے بعد جو مال باقی بچے اس تمام کی وصیت کر جائے (شامی ص ۴۱۸، ج ۵)
تنبیه ۲ اگر در بی مستامن اپنے سارے مال کی وصیت کر دے تو جائز ہے کیونکہ در شہ ندارد میں (فقد برم (شامی ۳۱۵ ، ج ۵)
The Book of Wills. A person has exactly as much property as he owes in debt; making a will for him is not permissible. He (al-Quduri) said: 'And whoever makes a will while he has debt that encompasses his wealth, the will is not valid, because debt takes precedence over the will, for it is the more important of the two needs: it is an obligation, while the will is a donation, and always the more important is given precedence, unless the creditors discharge him, for then no debt remains, so the will becomes effective to the extent prescribed due to his need for it.' Explanation: If a person has debt that encompasses his wealth, making a will is not permissible, because debt takes precedence over the will. This discussion has passed in the beginning of al-Sirajiyyah. Then the more important need between debt and will is the repayment of debt, because repaying debt is an obligation and the will is merely a donation, and the established principle is that the more important is given precedence, then step by step. If the creditors discharge the debtor from debt, then his will becomes permissible because the obstacle has been removed; all that is permissible will now be valid because the testator needs the will, which has been explained at the beginning of the Book of Wills. Note 1: For one who has no heir and no debt, it is preferable to give charity with his own hand and then make a will for all remaining wealth (Shami, vol. 5, p. 418). Note 2: If a musta'min (protected non-Muslim) makes a will for all his wealth, it is valid because he has no heirs (Shami, vol. 5, p. 315).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf Ul Hadaya explains the ruling from al-Quduri's matn: if a person has debt that covers all his assets, his will is invalid. The reasoning is based on prioritization: debt repayment is a fard (obligation), whereas a will is a tabarru' (voluntary donation). The principle is that the more important obligation is dealt with first. Therefore, the creditor's claims take precedence over the testator's wishes. However, if the creditors forgive the debt, the obstruction is removed and the will becomes valid to the extent that Islamic law permits. The text also adds two clarifications: (1) for a person with no heirs and no debt, it is better to give charity directly and then bequeath the rest; (2) a musta'min (non-Muslim under protection) can bequeath all his property because he has no Muslim heirs. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah as recorded in al-Quduri's concise text, which is authoritative in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest or will, a transfer of ownership after death, considered a tabarru' (voluntary donation).
دين (dayn): Debt owed by the testator, which is a prior claim on the estate before any will.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary act of giving without compensation; a will is a tabarru'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a person with debt encompassing his entire estate makes a will, but his creditors later forgive all the debts?
A) The will remains invalid because it was made while the debt existed.
B) The will becomes valid because the obstacle (debt) has been removed.
C) The will is valid only if the testator re-declares it after forgiveness.
D) The will is valid only to one-third of the freed amount.

Key Takeaway
Debt repayment is a binding obligation; a will is voluntary. When debt consumes the entire estate, the will is void, but creditor forgiveness can restore its validity.

Validity of a Minor's Will: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Presents the intra-madhhab dispute on the validity of a minor's will and rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a minor's will, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and apply the Hanafi reinterpretation of the evidence.

Core Idea
A minor's will is invalid due to lack of capacity for donation; the 'Umar precedent is reinterpreted to align with Hanafi principles.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the will of a minor. While the Shafi'i school permits a minor's will for virtuous deeds, the Hanafi school firmly restricts it, grounding its ruling on the principle that a minor lacks legal capacity for voluntary donations (tabarru'). We will explore both positions and the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proofs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis
Identify that the Hanafi school rules the will of a minor as invalid (la tasihh). Understand that the basis is that a minor is not qualified for tabarru' and his speech is not binding.
The core principle: legal capacity (ahliyyah) is required for donations.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its proofs
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the will valid if directed towards good deeds. Note his two proofs: the precedent of 'Umar permitting a minor's will, and the consideration of kindness to the minor in earning reward.
The Shafi'i view is based on istihsan (juristic preference) and a reported incident.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to each Shafi'i proof
Examine how the Hanafi school responds to the 'Umar incident by interpreting it either as the minor being near puberty (thus having some capacity) or the will being for funeral arrangements only, which is also allowed in the Hanafi school. Also, note the Hanafi argument that leaving wealth to heirs brings greater reward.
The Hanafi response demonstrates the method of takhsis (specification) and ta'wil (interpretation).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Understand that the practical difference is that Hanafis do not execute a minor's will except for funeral expenses of a discerning minor, while Shafi'is execute such wills if for virtuous purposes.
This dispute affects actual legal practice in estate distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قال ولا تصح وصية الصبى وقال الشافعى تصح اذا كان فى وجوه الخير لان عمر رضی الله عنه اجازوصية خ او يافع وهو الذى راهق الحلم ولانه نظر له بصرفه الى نفسه فى نيل الزلفى ولو لم تنفذ يبقى على غيره

تشریح یہ ہماری دلیل ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ بچہ تبرع کا اہل نہیں ہے حالانکہ وصیت تبرع ہے اور سری بات یہ ہے کہ بچہ کا قول ایسا نہیں ہوتا جو اس پر کوئی چیز لازم کر دے اور اگر اس کی وصیت کو صحیح مان لیا جائے تو اس کے قول کا ملزم ہونا لازم آئے گا اور یہ باطل ہے۔
رہا اثر عمر تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ وہ بچہ ابھی بالغ ہوا تھا تو حجاز کے چوبیس علاقوں میں سے علاقہ ما کان علیہ کہ وجہ سے مجازاً اس کو یفاع کہ دیا گیا ورنہ حقیقت میں وہ بالغ تھا یا اس کی تاویل یہ کی جائے کہ اس کی وصیت امر کفن دفن کے سلسلہ میں تھی اور یہ وصیت ہمارے نزدیک بھی جائز ہے اور رہی بات ثواب کی تو زیادہ ثواب مال کو اپنے ورثہ کے پاس چھوڑنے میں ہے لہذا وصیت باطل ہوگی۔
تنبیہ مجاز کے علاقوں کو ہم تفصیل سے الکلام المنظم میں بیان کر چکے ہیں ، اور امام شافعی کو اس کا تفصیلی جواب ہم درس حسامی میں دے چکے ہیں، اور اس جواب مذکور کی خامیاں دیکھنے کے لئے شامی ص ۴۲۰، ج ۵ ملاحظہ فرمائی جائے نیز بچہ کی وصیت امر دفن وکفن میں کہاں جائز ہے اور کہاں نہیں اس کی تفصیل شامی میں موجود ہے۔
The ruling of a minor's will and Imam al-Shafi'i's view and two proofs. He (al-Quduri) said: 'And the will of a minor is not valid.' Al-Shafi'i said: 'It is valid when it is for virtuous deeds, because 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) permitted the will of a lad near puberty, and because it is kindness to him, as he spends it for himself in seeking nearness to Allah; if it is not executed, the property remains with others.' Translation: Al-Quduri said: The will of a minor is not valid. Al-Shafi'i said: It is valid when it is for virtuous deeds, because 'Umar permitted the will of a boy approaching puberty, and because it is kindness to him, spending for himself to gain Allah's pleasure; if the will is not executed, his wealth remains with others. Explanation: There is a disagreement between us and al-Shafi'i: according to us it is not valid; according to him it is valid if for good causes. He gave two proofs: (1) 'Umar allowed the will of his son who was near puberty, named 'Amr ibn Sulaym; (2) it is kindness to the minor because otherwise his wealth is used by others, but if the will is executed, it is as if he spent it himself and gains reward. Our proof: We have (the argument) that it is a donation and a minor is not qualified for donations, and his words are not binding; to validate his will requires considering his words binding; the reported incident is interpreted (mahmul) as being metaphorically near puberty, or the will was about his own shroud and burial, which is valid in our school, and the minor acquires reward by leaving (the wealth) to his heirs, as we have explained. Explanation: This is our proof: the minor is not qualified for tabarru' (donation), and his speech does not create obligations. If we uphold his will, we would be saying his speech is binding, which is false. As for the reported incident of 'Umar, it is understood either that the boy was actually near puberty (in which case he was metaphorically called a minor, but was actually of sound judgment) or that the will pertained to funeral arrangements, which is permitted in our school. As for reward, leaving wealth to heirs earns more reward. Therefore, the will is invalid. Note: We have discussed the metaphorical usage in detail in al-Kalam al-Munazzam, and we gave a detailed response to al-Shafi'i in Dars Hisami. For criticism of this response, see Shami, vol. 5, p. 420. Also, details of where a minor's will for burial and shroud is valid and where not are found in Shami.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage discusses a classic inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position, as stated by al-Quduri, is that the will of a minor (sabi) is not valid. Imam al-Shafi'i argues it is valid if directed towards good deeds, citing the precedent of Caliph 'Umar allowing the will of a minor near puberty (yafi') and arguing that it benefits the child by earning him reward. The Hanafi response, presented in the text, addresses both proofs: (1) Minors do not have the legal capacity (ahliyyah) for tabarru' (voluntary donations) because their speech is not binding (ghayr mulzim). Upholding their will would imply their speech is binding, which is false. (2) The incident of 'Umar is interpreted either as the minor actually being close to puberty (thus having some discretion) or as the will concerning funeral arrangements only, which is allowed in the Hanafi school. (3) The reward argument is turned around: leaving wealth to one's heirs brings more reward than giving it away as a will. Thus, the Hanafi school maintains the invalidity of a minor's will. The passage belongs to Zahir al-Riwayah as it is from the core texts. The mufta bihi position is that a minor's will is invalid except for funeral arrangements (tajhiz and dafn) if he is of discerning age (sabi mumayyiz).

Key Terms
وصية الصبي (wasiyyat al-sabi): The will of a minor (pre-puberty child) who is not legally responsible.
تبرع (tabarru'): A voluntary donation requiring legal capacity; a minor cannot perform valid tabarru'.
يفاع / يافع (yafi' / yafi'): A lad approaching puberty (muhaq), close to the age of discernment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis reject the Shafi'i proof from 'Umar's approval of a minor's will?
A) Because 'Umar never actually approved such a will.
B) Because the minor in question was actually an adult or the will was for funeral matters, not general bequest.
C) Because the minor was a non-Muslim.
D) Because the will was for a prohibited purpose.

Key Takeaway
A minor's will is invalid in the Hanafi school due to lack of legal capacity for donation, except for funeral matters of a discerning minor.

Wasiyyah — Bequest

The Invalidity of a Child's Bequest: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Hanafi rebuttal of Shafi'i's argument for child's bequest validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school invalidates a child's bequest and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of wad' al-tasarruf.

Core Idea
A child's bequest is void because the nature of the transaction, not its occasional benefit, determines legal capacity.

Imam al-Shafi'i argued that a child's bequest should be valid because it can benefit the child. The Hanafi school responds with a powerful methodological principle: we look at the inherent nature of transactions (wad' al-tasarruf), not at coincidental outcomes. This lesson unpacks that principle and its application to the child's bequest, using the analogy of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Ruling
State the Hanafi position: a child's bequest is absolutely invalid (batil), whether immediate or conditional.
The ruling is based on lack of ahliyyah (legal capacity).
2
Understand the Opponent's Argument
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that since a child can benefit from a bequest, it should be valid occasionally.
This is a common-sense argument focusing on outcome.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Principle
The Hanafi response introduces the principle of wad' al-tasarruf: we consider the essential nature of the transaction, not accidental benefits.
Bequest is a form of ownership; a child lacks capacity to own such a transaction.
4
Follow the Analogy to Divorce
Divorce is inherently harmful even if it occasionally brings benefit. Similarly, a child does not own the power to divorce or make a bequest.
This is a qiyas (analogy) from divorce to bequest.
5
Distinguish from the Slave's Case
A slave or mukatib has full capacity; the obstacle is the master's right. Thus a deferred bequest after manumission is valid.
This contrast reinforces that the child's deficiency is in capacity itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتبر فى النفع والضرر النظر الى اوضاع التصرفات لا الى ما يتفق بحكم الحال اعتبره بالطلاق فانه لا يملكه ولا وصية وان كان يتفق نافعا فى بعض الاحوال وكذا اوصى ثم مات بعد الادراك بعدم الاهلية وقت المباشرة وكذا اذا قال اذا ادرکت فثلث مالی لفلان وصية لقصور اهليته فلا يملكه تنجيزا وتعليقا کمافی الطلاق والعتاق بخلاف العبد والمكاتب لان اهليتهما مستتمة و المانع حق المولى فتصح أضافته الى حال سقوطه
And the consideration in benefit and harm is to look at the natures of transactions, not at what happens coincidentally by circumstance. Analogize it to divorce: the child does not own it nor a bequest, even if it is occasionally beneficial. And likewise, if he made a bequest then died after puberty, [it is invalid] due to lack of capacity at the time of direct action. And likewise if he said: 'When I reach puberty, one-third of my wealth is for so-and-so as a bequest' – due to the deficiency of his capacity, he does not own it as an immediate or conditional [transaction], as in divorce and manumission. Contrary to the slave and the mukatib, because their capacity is complete, and the obstacle is the right of the master, so attributing it to a state when [the right] falls is valid.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage addresses Imam Shafi'i's argument that a child's bequest should be valid because it benefits the child. The Hanafi response establishes a key principle: in determining benefit or harm, one must look at the inherent nature of the transaction (wad' al-tasarruf), not at occasional coincidental benefits. The example of divorce (talaq) is given: divorce is inherently harmful (mudirr), even if occasionally it leads to benefit (e.g., marrying a wealthier sister). Thus a child or his guardian cannot own the power to divorce. Similarly, a bequest is a form of ownership. Since a child lacks complete capacity, his bequest is invalid whether immediate or conditional. This is contrasted with a slave or mukatib, whose capacity is complete but whose owner's right prevents donation. When they attribute a bequest to a future time when the right of the master falls (e.g., after manumission), it is valid.

Key Terms
وضع التصرف (wad' al-tasarruf): The inherent nature or essential characteristic of a legal transaction, as opposed to accidental outcomes.
اهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity to perform transactions; a child has deficient capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a child's bequest invalid in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the child cannot own property
B) Because the child lacks legal capacity to own the transaction of bequest
C) Because bequests are always harmful
D) Because the child's guardian does not allow it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a child's bequest is void because the essential nature of the transaction requires full capacity, regardless of any potential benefit.

Ruling on Mukatib's Bequest and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a mukatib's bequest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a mukatib's bequest and identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
A mukatib's bequest is invalid unless conditioned on future freedom—according to the Sahibayn, capacity overcomes the property obstacle.

This lesson examines a well-known intra-Hanafi disagreement concerning the validity of a bequest made by a mukatib (a slave under a contract of manumission). While the matn of al-Quduri states the bequest is invalid, a different view attributed to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) considers it valid by analogy to a conditional manumission. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists weigh capacity (ahliyyah) against property encumbrances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling in the matn
The matn states the mukatib's bequest is invalid. Note that this is the initial ruling without exceptions.
The matn does not mention conditions; it is a general statement.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The passage notes a disagreement: Abu Hanifa agrees with the matn (invalid), while the Sahibayn say it is valid.
All three imams agree the mukatib has partial capacity; the issue is the nature of his property.
3
Analyze the dalil for each position
Abu Hanifa: the mukatib's property does not accept donation (tabarru'). Sahibayn: analogy to a mukatib's conditional manumission of future slaves—the obstacle (master's right) can cease.
The analogy is key: a conditional bequest is attributed to a future state where the mukatib owns the property.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If the mukatib dies before freedom with an unconditional bequest, both agree it is invalid. If conditional, Sahibayn consider it valid if fulfilled; Abu Hanifa considers it invalid regardless.
The practical difference arises only when the bequest is conditional upon freedom.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and the tarjih
Later Hanafi jurists preferred the Sahibayn's view, making it the relied-upon position for fatwa.
This shows how intra-madhhab ikhtilaf is resolved through tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا تصح وصية المكاتب و ان ترك وفاء لان ماله لا يقبل التبرع وقيل على قول أبي حنيفة لا تصح وعندهما تصح رداً لها الى مكاتب يقول كل مملوک املكه فيما استقبل فهو حرثم عتق فملك والخلاف فيها معروف وعرف في موضعه
He said: And the bequest of a mukatib is not valid, even if he leaves sufficient [property to pay the kitabah], because his property does not accept donation. And it is said: According to Abu Hanifa, it is not valid; and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is valid, referring it to a mukatib who says: 'Every slave I own in the future is free.' Then he [the mukatib] was freed and [thus] owned [the slave]. And the disagreement concerning it is well-known and recognized in its place.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a mukatib's bequest. The matn (by al-Quduri) states outright that a mukatib's bequest is invalid, even if he leaves enough property to pay off his kitabah (contract of manumission), because his property cannot be donated (tabarru'). However, a different view is attributed: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, it is invalid; according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad), it is valid. They draw an analogy to the case where a mukatib says, 'Every slave I own in the future is free.' If the mukatib later becomes free and acquires slaves, that conditional manumission is effective. Similarly, if a mukatib makes a bequest conditional upon his freedom, it should be valid because his capacity is complete at the time of making the bequest, though the obstacle (the master's right) prevents immediate effectiveness. The dispute is well-known and fully discussed in other works (e.g., al-Jami' al-Kabir, Kitab al-Iman).

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, agreeing to pay a sum in exchange for freedom.
تبرع (tabarru'): Donation or gratuitous transfer of property without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is a mukatib's bequest valid?
A) Because the mukatib owns all his property absolutely
B) By analogy to a mukatib who says 'Every slave I own in the future is free'
C) Because the master's right is irrelevant
D) Because a bequest is not a donation

Key Takeaway
The mukatib's bequest is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn's view (valid if conditional upon freedom) is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Bequest for a Fetus: Conditions and Hanafi Reasoning
Ruling on the validity of bequests made for or by a fetus, with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid bequest to and by a fetus and explain the Hanafi analogy to inheritance and to non-existent objects.

Core Idea
A bequest for a fetus is valid if born within six months, as bequest mirrors inheritance.

This lesson explores a unique ruling in Islamic inheritance law: the validity of bequests involving an unborn child (haml). While a fetus is not yet a legal person, the Hanafi school recognizes its potential rights. Through careful reasoning, the jurists establish that a bequest for a fetus (and even by a fetus) is valid under a specific condition. We will unpack the condition, the underlying analogy, and compare it to similar transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of bequests
The passage discusses two scenarios: a bequest made for the benefit of a fetus (e.g., 'I bequeath to the child in X's womb') and a bequest made by the fetus itself (i.e., the fetus is made a legatee). Both are subject to the same condition.
The second type is less common; it means the fetus is the recipient of the bequest.
2
State the condition for validity
The condition is that the fetus is born within six months from the time of the bequest. This interval is the minimum period of pregnancy, confirming that the fetus existed at the time the bequest was made.
If born after six months, the bequest is invalid because the existence of the fetus at the time of bequest is uncertain.
3
Analyze the reasoning for bequest for a fetus
The Hanafi jurists argue that a bequest is a form of succession (istikhlaf), similar to inheritance. Since a fetus can inherit, it can also be the beneficiary of a bequest. However, unlike inheritance, a bequest requires acceptance after death; if the heir (the child) rejects it, it is void. This distinguishes it from a gift (hibah), which is pure ownership and requires immediate acceptance.
The fetus cannot accept at the time of the will, so the bequest is valid contingent on acceptance after birth.
4
Analyze the reasoning for bequest by a fetus
This part is explained by the fact that the fetus is 'subject to existence' (bi-'arad al-wujud) if born within six months. The rules of bequest are more flexible due to the testator's need and incapacity, so bequests are even valid for non-existent things (e.g., fruit of a tree). Therefore, a bequest for an existing thing (the fetus) is even more appropriate.
This is a stronger argument (a fortiori): if valid for non-existent, then for existing it is more valid.
5
Contrast with gift (hibah)
The passage explicitly contrasts a bequest with a gift. A gift to a fetus is invalid because it requires immediate delivery of ownership, and no one has guardianship to accept on the fetus's behalf at the time of the gift. A bequest, however, does not require immediate transfer; it is effective only after death, when the child is born and can accept.
This distinction highlights the importance of the nature of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وتجوز الوصية للحمل وبالحمل اذا وضع لا قل من ستة اشهر من وقت الوصية اما الأول فلان الوصية استخلاف من وجه لانه يجعله خليفة في بعض ماله والجنين صلح خليفة فى الارث فكذا في الوصية اذاهي اخته الا انه يرتد بالرد لما فيه من معنى التملیك بخلاف الهبة لانها تملیك محض ولا ولاية لاحد عليه ليملكه شيئا واما الثانى فلانه بعرض الوجود اذ الكلام فيما اذا علم وجوده وقت الوصية وبابها أوسع لحاجة الميت وعجزه وهذا تصح في غير الموجود كالثمرة فلان تصح في الموجود الأولى
He said: And the bequest for the fetus and by the fetus is valid when it is born less than six months from the time of the bequest. As for the first (bequest for the fetus): because the bequest is a form of succession (istikhlaf) in a way, as it makes him a successor in some of his wealth. And the fetus is suitable as a successor in inheritance, so likewise in bequest, for it is the sister of inheritance, except that it is rejected by rejection (radd) because it contains the meaning of ownership (tamlik). Contrary to hibah (gift), because it is pure ownership, and no one has guardianship over him to make him own anything. And as for the second (bequest by the fetus): because he is subject to existence, since the discussion is when his existence is known at the time of the bequest, and its scope is wider due to the need of the deceased and his incapacity. And this is valid for a non-existent thing like fruit, so that it is valid for an existing thing is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage discusses two types of bequests involving a fetus (haml): (1) a bequest made for the benefit of a fetus (e.g., 'I bequeath one-third of my wealth to the child in so-and-so's womb'), and (2) a bequest made by the fetus itself (e.g., a fetus is made the executor or is the subject of a bequest, though technically the fetus cannot speak; the meaning is that a bequest is made contingent upon the child being born). The condition for both is that the fetus is born within six months from the time of the bequest, proving its existence at the time of the bequest (since the minimum period of pregnancy is six months). The reasoning for (1): a bequest is a kind of succession (istikhlaf), like inheritance. A fetus can be an heir, so it can also be a beneficiary of a bequest. However, a bequest has an element of ownership (tamlik), so it is invalid if the fetus rejects it after birth (by not accepting). This differs from hibah (gift), which is pure ownership and cannot be made to a fetus since no one has guardianship to accept ownership on its behalf (a bequest does not require acceptance at the time of the will, only a condition). For (2): the fetus is considered 'subject to existence' (bi-'arad al-wujud) because its existence is known at the time of the will if it is born within six months. The scope of bequests is wider than other transactions due to the need of the dying person, so bequests can even be made for non-existent things like fruit of a tree, so making a bequest for an existing thing (the fetus) is even more appropriate.

Key Terms
استخلاف (istikhlaf): Making someone a successor in property; a bequest is a kind of succession similar to inheritance.
حمل (haml): Fetus in the womb; a potential legal person whose existence is contingent on birth within six months.
رد (radd): Rejection; a bequest can be rejected by the legatee after the testator's death, unlike inheritance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must be met for a bequest for a fetus to be valid?
A) The fetus must be born within nine months
B) The fetus must be born within six months of the bequest
C) The fetus must be male
D) The bequest must be accepted by the guardian at the time of the will

Key Takeaway
A bequest for a fetus is valid if the child is born within six months, because bequest is analogous to inheritance and valid for non-existent objects.
