Validity of a Mukatab's Will: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mukatab's will validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a mukatab's will and explain the qiyas supporting the Sahibayn's view.

Core Idea
A mukatab's will is invalid per Abu Hanifa, but valid per Sahibayn via qiyas on conditional manumission.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of a will made by a mukatab (a slave under a kitaba contract). The base text states the will is invalid, but this is only the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. His two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that it is valid, drawing an analogy to a mukatab's conditional manumission. Understanding this dispute reveals how qiyas operates within the Hanafi school even on fundamental issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What is the ruling in the base text? Who disagrees? The base text (al-Quduri) states that a mukatab's will is invalid even if he leaves enough wealth. This is Abu Hanifa's view. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is valid.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school itself.
2
State Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that a mukatab's wealth does not accept donation (tabarru') because he is still a slave. Therefore, his will (a form of tabarru') is invalid.
This is based on the principle that a mukatab lacks full capacity for gratuitous dispositions.
3
State the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn analogize (qiyas) to a case where a mukatab says: 'Every slave I own in the future is free.' If he later gains freedom and ownership, that conditional statement takes effect. Similarly, a will made during kitaba becomes effective after freedom because then he owns property.
The analogy hinges on the mukatab's future capacity to own property.
4
Analyze the qiyas in detail
Identify the asl (original case), far' (new case), and illa (effective cause). Use the qiyas panel below to map out the reasoning.
The text notes that even the analogy itself is debated, as recorded in al-Jami' al-Kabir.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: If a mukatab makes a will and later gains freedom, the will is valid according to the Sahibayn but invalid according to Abu Hanifa. A mufti must know which view to follow.
The mufta bihi is not specified in this text, so the dispute remains unresolved in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا تصح وصية المكاتب و ان ترك وفاء لان ماله لا يقبل التبرع وقيل على قول أبي حنيفة لا تصح وعندهما تصح رداً لها الى مكاتب يقول كل مملوک املكه فيما استقبل فهو حرثم عتق فملك والخلاف فيها معروف وعرف في موضعه
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور مکاتب کی وصیت صحیح نہیں ہے اگر چہ وفا چھوڑا ہو اس لئے کہ اس کا مال تتبرع کو قبول نہیں کرتا اور کہا گیا ہے کہ ابو حنیفہ کے قول کے مطابق صحیح نہیں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک صحیح ہے وصیت کو لوٹاتے ہوئے ایسے مکاتب کی جانب جو کہتا ہے کہ ہر مملوک جس کا میں مالک ہو جاؤں آئندہ زمانہ میں پس وہ آزاد ہے پھر مکاتب آزاد کیا گیا پھر وہ مالک ہوا اور اس میں اختلاف معروف ہے جس کو اس کے مقام پر پہچان کیا گیا ہے۔ تشریح مکاتب کی تعلیقاً وصیت تو جائز ہے کمامر لیکن تجیز ا اس کی وصیت جائز نہیں ہے اگر وہ بدل کتابت کے برابر بھی مال چھوڑ کر مرے تب بھی اس کی وصیت صحیح نہ ہوگی اس لئے کہ مکاتب کا مال تبرع کو قبول ہی نہیں کرتا یہی تو وجہ ہے کہ اس کا عشق اور مبہ صحیح نہیں ہے۔ اور بعض حضرات نے یہ کہا ہے کہ مکاتب کی وصیت امام ابوحنیفہ کے قول مطابق صحیح نہیں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک صیح ہے، صاحبین نے اس کو اس مسئلہ پر قیاس کیا ہے جب کہ کوئی مکاتب کہے کہ میں آئندہ زمانہ میں جس مملوک کا مالک ہو جاؤں تو وہ آزاد ہے پھر یہ مکاتب آزاد ہو گیا اور مالک بنا تو یہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ حالانکہ صاحبین نے قیاس میں جو جزئیہ پیش کیا ہے اس میں بھی اختلاف ہے جس کی تفصیل جامع کبیر کی کتاب الایمان میں ہے۔ نے قیاس میں جو ہےاس میں ہے کی اور مکاتب کی وصیت کی مزید تفصیلات کے لئے مجمع الانہار اور شامی ملاحظہ فرمائیں۔
He (al-Quduri) said: The will of a mukatab is not valid, even if he leaves enough (to pay the kitaba), because his wealth does not accept donation (tabarru'). And it is said that according to Abu Hanifa's opinion it is not valid, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is valid, returning (relying) on the statement of a mukatab who says: 'Every slave that I will own in the future is free,' then he is manumitted, then he owns (property). The difference is well-known and is recognized in its place. Translation: al-Quduri said: The will of a mukatab is not valid, even if he leaves enough (to pay the kitaba), because his wealth does not accept donation. And it has been said that according to the statement of Abu Hanifa it is not valid, and according to the two (Sahibayn) it is valid, returning the will to the mukatab who says: 'Every slave I own in the future is free,' then the mukatab is freed, then he becomes an owner. This difference is well-known and is recognized in its place. Explanation: A suspended will (ta'liqan) from a mukatab is valid, as is the case (of manumission), but an immediate will (tanjizan) is not valid. Even if he dies leaving wealth equal to the kitaba, his will is still not valid because the wealth of a mukatab does not accept donation. This is the reason why his (immediate) will is not valid. And some scholars said that the will of a mukatab is not valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement, and according to the two (Sahibayn) it is valid. The two analogized this to the case when a mukatab says: 'Every slave I own in the future is free,' then he is freed and becomes an owner, so that slave becomes free. However, even in the analogy that the two presented, there is difference, the details of which are in al-Jami' al-Kabir in Kitab al-Iman. For more details on the will of a mukatab, refer to Majma' al-Anhar and al-Shami.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of a mukatab's will. The base text (al-Quduri) states that a mukatab's will is invalid even if he leaves enough wealth to pay the kitaba, because his wealth is not legally capable of being subject to donation (tabarru'). However, the commentary clarifies that this is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that it is valid. Their reasoning is based on analogy (qiyas) with a scenario where a mukatab makes a conditional statement: 'Every slave I come to own in the future is free.' If he is later freed and becomes an owner, that slave is freed. By analogy, once the mukatab is freed and owns property, his will becomes effective. Abu Hanifa restricts the ability to make donations while in the state of kitaba. The commentary notes that even in the analogy there is disagreement, referenced to al-Jami' al-Kabir. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here, but the difference is acknowledged.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to be freed upon paying a sum (kitaba). He is partially free while in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) If the mukatab leaves more than the kitaba amount
D) If the will is for a specific category of heirs

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's will is disputed within the Hanafi school: invalid per Abu Hanifa, valid per Sahibayn by qiyas on conditional manumission.

Validity of a Will for or by a Fetus
Establishing validity of will for/by a fetus with condition and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a will for or by a fetus, explain the proof using qiyas on inheritance, and distinguish it from a gift.

Core Idea
A will for a fetus is valid like inheritance, but a gift is invalid because a fetus cannot receive ownership directly.

This passage from al-Quduri establishes that a will in favor of a fetus or concerning a fetus is valid on the condition that the child is born within six months. The reasoning relies on the analogy between will and inheritance, and the distinction from a gift. This lesson unpacks the legal proof and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Condition
State clearly that the Hanafi position is that a will for a fetus and a will by a fetus are both valid (sahih) provided the child is born within six months of the will. This condition confirms the fetus existed at the time of the will.
The six-month window is based on the minimum period of pregnancy according to Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the Qiyas (Analogy) with Inheritance
The author argues that a will is like inheritance (istikhlaf) because both involve the deceased appointing a successor to receive property. Since a fetus can inherit, it can also be the beneficiary of a will. The will is called the 'sister of inheritance'.
This analogy is the core proof for the ruling.
3
Distinguish Will from Gift (Hiba)
A gift is pure ownership (tamlik mahd) requiring immediate transfer and acceptance by a guardian. Since a fetus has no guardian for acceptance, a gift to a fetus is invalid. A will, however, takes effect only after death and does not require immediate acceptance, so it is valid.
This distinction highlights that the will is a form of succession, not immediate ownership.
4
Apply the Wider Scope of Wills
The door of will is wider because of the testator's need and incapacity. Just as a will for non-existent future fruit is valid, a will for an existing fetus is even more valid. This reinforces the validity for the fetus whose existence is known.
This principle shows the leniency of Shariah in testamentary matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وتجوز الوصية للحمل وبالحمل اذا وضع لا قل من ستة اشهر من وقت الوصية اما الأول فلان الوصية استخلاف من وجه لانه يجعله خليفة في بعض ماله والجنين صلح خليفة فى الارث فكذا في الوصية اذاهي اخته الا انه يرتد بالرد لما فيه من معنى التملیک بخلاف الهبة لانها تملیک محض ولا ولاية لاحد عليه ليملكه شيئا واما الثانى فلانه بعرض الوجود اذ الكلام فيما اذا علم وجوده وقت الوصية وبابها أوسع لحاجة الميت وعجزه وهذا تصح في غير الموجود كالثمرة فلان تصح في الموجود الأولى
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور حمل کے لئے اور حمل کی وصیت جائز ہے جب کہ وہ وصیت کے وقت سے چھ مہینے کم میں پیدا ہو جائے بہر حال پس اس لئے کہ وصیت ایک اعتبار سے استخلاف ہے اپنے بعض مال کے اندر اور جنین میراث میں خلیفہ ہونے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے پس ایسے ہی وصیت کے اندر اس لئے کہ وصیت میراث کی بہن ہے مگر وصیت زد کرنے سے زد ہو جاتی ہے اس وجہ سے کہ اس میں تملیک کے معنی ہیں بخلاف ہبہ کے اس لئے کہ یہ محض تملیک ہے اور جنین پر کسی کو ولایت نہیں ہے جو اس کو کسی چیز کا مالک بنا دے، بہر حال ثانی پس اس لئے کہ جنین وجود کے پیش آنے کے مقام پر ہے اس لئے کہ گفتگو اس صورت میں ہے جب کہ وصیت کے وقت اس کا وجود معلوم ہو جائے اور میت کی حاجت اور اس کی عاجزی کی وجہ سے وصیت کا باب بہت وسیع ہے اس وجہ سے غیر موجود میں وصیت صحیح ہے جیسے پھلوں میں پس موجود میں بدرجہ اولیٰ وصیت صحیح ہوگی۔
تشریح اگر کوئی بچہ پیٹ میں ہے اور اس کے کسی مورث کا انتقال ہو جائے تو حمل کو بھی میراث ملے گی ، اور میراث اور وصیت دیا کہ دونوں بہنیں ہیں لہذا حمل کے لئے وصیت کی جائے تو جائز ہے اور اسی طرح اگر حمل ہی کی وصیت کر دی جائے تو وہ بھی جائزہ ہے مثلا کہے کہ میری باندی کے پیٹ میں جو بچہ ہے یہ فلاں شخص کو دے دینا تو یہ بھی صحیح ہے اور وصیت جائز ہے ۔ لیکن یہ وصیت حمل کیلئے یا حمل کی اس وقت جائز ہے جب کہ حمل پیٹ میں موجود ہو جس کی علامت یہ ہے کہ وصیت کے وقت سے چھ مہینے سے کم میں بچہ پیدا ہو جائے ۔ پھر مصنف نے ان دونوں کی الگ الگ دلیل بیان فرمائی ۔ حمل کے لئے وصیت جائز ہے اس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وصیت فی الحال مالک بنانا نہیں ہے بلکہ میراث کی طرح اس میں بھی استخلاف ہے تو جیسے میراث جائز ہے اسی طرح وصیت بھی جائز ہے کیونکہ وصیت و میراث آپس میں ایک دوسرے کی نظیریں ہیں۔ بس اتنا فرق ہے کہ وصیت رد کرنے سے رد ہو جائے گی اور میراث رد نہیں ہوگی کیونکہ وصیت میں من جانب موصی تملیک کا پہلو ہے اور جہاں تملیک کا پہلو ہو وہاں رجوع جائز ہے اور میراث میں تملیک نہیں ہے بلکہ محض استخلاف ہے۔ حمل کے لئے وصیت تو جائز ہے لیکن حمل کے لئے بہہ جائز نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ ہبہ خالص تملیک ہے جس میں استخلاف نہیں ہے اور چونکہ حمل ابھی پیدا نہیں ہوا تو اس پر ابھی کسی کو ولایت حاصل نہیں ہوسکی جو اس کو کسی چیز کا مالک بنا سکے اس لئے ہبہ نا جائز ہے۔ اور رہی عمل کی وصیت تو وہ بھی جائز ہے اس لئے کہ ہماری گفتگو ایسے جنین حمل کے بارے میں ہے جس کا بوقت وصیت موجود ہونا معلوم ہو اور جب یہاں اس کا موجود ہونا معلوم ہو چکا ہے تو وہ جنین چند ایام کے بعد موجود ہو ہی جائے گا۔ اور میت کی حاجت و بجز کے پیش نظر باب وصیت وسیع ہے اور وسعت کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے یہ وصیت کی کہ میرے باغ میں جو پھل آئے گا یہ فلاں کے لئے وصیت ہے تو یہ وصیت جائز ہے حالانکہ پھل ابھی موجود نہیں تو جو چیز موجود ہے اس کی وصیت بدرجہ اولیٰ جائز ہے۔
He (al-Quduri) said: The will for a fetus (li'l-haml) and by a fetus (bi'l-haml) is valid when it (the child) is born less than six months from the time of the will. As for the first (will for a fetus): because a will is a form of succession (istikhlaf) in a way; he makes him a successor in part of his wealth, and a fetus is suitable to be a successor in inheritance, so similarly in will, since it (will) is the sister of inheritance. However, it is revoked by rejection (radd) because it has the meaning of ownership (tamlik), unlike a gift (hiba) because that is pure ownership, and no one has guardianship over the fetus to make it own something. As for the second (will by a fetus): because it is on the verge of existence, since we are speaking about a case where its existence is known at the time of the will, and the door of will is wider due to the need of the deceased and his incapacity. Therefore, a will is valid for non-existent things like fruit, so it is more fitting that it should be valid for an existing thing. Translation: al-Quduri said: The will for a fetus and the will by a fetus is valid when it is born less than six months from the time of the will. As for the first: because a will is a form of succession in a way; he makes him a successor in some of his wealth, and a fetus is suitable to be a successor in inheritance, so similarly in will because it is the sister of inheritance, except that it is repudiated by rejection because it has the meaning of ownership, unlike a gift because that is pure ownership and no one has guardianship over the fetus to make it own anything. As for the second: because it is on the verge of existence, as we speak of a case where its existence is known at the time of will, and the door of will is wider due to the need of the deceased and his incapacity. Thus, a will is valid for non-existent things like fruit, so it is more fitting that it be valid for existent things. Explanation: If a child is in the womb and any ancestor of his dies, the fetus also receives inheritance. Since inheritance and will are sisters, therefore making a will for a fetus is valid. Similarly, making a will by a fetus (i.e., bequeathing something the fetus owns?) is also valid. For example, one says: 'The child in my slave-girl's womb is to be given to so-and-so.' This is correct and the will is valid. But this will for or by a fetus is valid only when the fetus is present in the womb, the sign of which is that the child is born within less than six months from the time of the will. Then the author gave separate proofs for both. The proof that a will for a fetus is valid is that a will is not immediate ownership but like inheritance it involves succession. Just as inheritance is valid, so is a will, because will and inheritance are counterparts. The only difference is that a will can be revoked by rejection, but inheritance cannot be revoked because in a will there is an aspect of ownership from the testator, and where there is an aspect of ownership, revocation is permissible. In inheritance there is no ownership but pure succession. A will for a fetus is valid, but a gift (hiba) for a fetus is not valid because a gift is pure ownership without succession, and since the fetus is not yet born, no one has guardianship to make it own something, so a gift is invalid. As for a will by a fetus (bi'l-haml), it is also valid because we are speaking of a fetus whose existence is known at the time of will, and since its existence is known, it will exist after a few days. The door of will is wide due to the need and incapacity of the deceased. The proof that the door is wide is that if someone makes a will saying: 'The fruit that will come in my garden is for so-and-so,' this will is valid even though the fruit does not exist yet. So for something that exists, it is all the more valid.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage establishes two rulings: (1) It is valid to make a will in favor of a fetus (i.e., bequeathing something to a child in the womb), and (2) it is valid to make a will concerning a fetus (i.e., bequeathing a fetus itself—such as a slave-girl's unborn child—to someone). Both are valid on the condition that the child is born within six months of the will, which proves it existed at the time. The reasoning: A will is similar to inheritance in that both involve appointing a successor to receive assets after death. Since a fetus can inherit, it can also be the beneficiary of a will. However, a will differs from inheritance in that it can be revoked because it contains an element of ownership (tamlik). This is why a gift (hiba) to a fetus is not valid: gift is pure ownership requiring immediate delivery and guardianship, which the fetus does not have. For a will concerning the fetus (e.g., bequeathing the unborn child itself), it is valid because the fetus is on the verge of existence and the testator's needs justify a broader scope: just as a will for future fruit is valid, so is a will for an existing entity like a known fetus. The passage thus clarifies the Hanafi position on testamentary dispositions involving unborn children.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): A fetus in the womb, considered legally for succession and will purposes.
استخلاف (istikhlāf): Appointing someone as successor; a will is seen as a form of succession (like inheritance) rather than immediate transfer of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a will for a fetus valid but a gift to a fetus is not, according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because a gift requires immediate possession and guardianship, which a fetus lacks
B) Because a will is a contract of sale
C) Because a fetus cannot own property
D) Because the testator can always change a will

Key Takeaway
A will for or by a fetus is valid if born within six months, because a will is like succession (istikhlaf), not immediate ownership.

Exception of the Fetus in a Will of a Pregnant Slave-Girl
Explaining the validity of bequeathing a pregnant slave-girl while excepting the fetus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on excepting a fetus from a bequest and apply the general principle that whatever can be independently bequeathed can be excepted.

Core Idea
What can be independently bequeathed can be excepted; the fetus qualifies.

In the previous lesson, you learned that a fetus can be the direct subject of a will. Here, the text builds on that by addressing an exception: what happens if the testator bequeaths the mother but wishes to exclude the unborn child? The ruling affirms its validity and extracts a general principle governing exceptions in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and ruling
State the scenario: a testator makes a will for a pregnant slave-girl but excepts the fetus. The ruling: both the bequest and the exception are valid (sahih).
2
Analyze the linguistic basis
The term 'slave-girl' (jāriya) does not include the fetus lexically; the fetus is only included by implication (taba'an) as a dependent. Therefore, when the mother is singled out, the fetus is not automatically included.
This is a type of istithnā' based on word meaning.
3
Trace the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
The asl (original case): bequeathing a pregnant slave-girl is valid. The far' (new case): excepting the fetus. The illa (effective cause): the fetus can be independently bequeathed (as established in earlier fard). Thus, the exception is valid analogically.
Qiyas jaliy: the ruling is derived from the fact that independent bequest is possible.
4
Extract the general principle
The passage concludes a rule: whatever can be validly singled out in a contract, its exception is valid; whatever cannot be singled out, its exception is invalid. This principle is drawn from the rules of sales (buyū') and applies to wills as well.
This shows the systematic integration of different chapters of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن أوصى بجارية الأحملها صحت الوصية والاستثناء لان اسم الجارية لايتناول الحمل لفظا ولكنه يستحق بالاطلاق تبعا فاذا افرد الام بالوصية صح افرادها ولانه يصح افراد الحمل بالوصية فجاز استثناء ه وهذا هو الاصل ان ما يصح افراده بالعقد يصح استثناء ة منه اذلا فرق بينهما وما لا يصح افراده بالعقد لا يصح استثناء ة منه وقد مرفى البيوع
He said: Whoever makes a will concerning a slave-girl with her pregnancy, the will and the exception are valid because the name 'slave-girl' does not include the fetus verbally, but it (the fetus) is included by implication (taba'an) as a dependent. So when the mother is singled out in the will, the singling out is valid. And because it is valid to make a will for the fetus separately, therefore excepting it is permissible. This is the principle: whatever can be validly singled out in a contract, its exception is valid, for there is no difference between them. And whatever cannot be validly singled out in a contract, its exception is not valid. This has been explained in the chapter on sales.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling and a general principle regarding exceptions in wills. The case: a testator bequeaths a pregnant slave-girl to someone, but excludes the unborn child. Both the bequest and the exception are valid. The reasoning: The term 'slave-girl' (jāriya) linguistically does not include the fetus; the fetus is only included as a dependent (taba'). Therefore, when the testator specifically bequeaths the mother alone, the exception is effectively implicit. Moreover, since it is permissible to bequeath a fetus independently (as established in the previous lesson), it is certainly permissible to except it. This leads to a general principle: In any contract, if an item can be the subject of that contract independently, then its exception from a broader item is also valid. Conversely, if an item cannot be the independent subject, its exception is invalid. This principle is drawn from the rules of sales (buyū'), showing the systematic nature of Hanafi fiqh. The passage reinforces the flexibility of wasiyya compared to other contracts like hiba.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā'): The legal act of excepting something from a bequest or contract, removing it from the scope of the disposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whatever can be validly singled out in a contract, its exception is valid.
B) Exception is only valid if explicitly stated.
C) Exception is never valid in wills.
D) The fetus cannot be excepted because it is part of the mother.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the validity of excepting an item from a bequest depends on whether that item can independently be the subject of a contract.

Bequest to a Fetus: Hanafi Validity and Qiyas with Inheritance
Ruling with proof using qiyas jaliy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequest to a fetus and explain the analogy (qiyas) with inheritance that justifies its validity.

Core Idea
A bequest to a fetus is valid because it is succession, not immediate ownership — just like inheritance.

Can you leave property to a child not yet born? In Hanafi fiqh, yes — through a bequest (wasiyyah). This lesson unpacks the unique reasoning: bequest is not an immediate gift but a future transfer, making it permissible for a fetus. We will explore the proof from analogy with inheritance and the distinction from a gift (hibah), which is invalid for a fetus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules that a bequest for a fetus (or of the fetus itself) is valid (sahih). Hibah (gift) to a fetus is invalid. Identify the hukm level and the condition that the fetus must exist at the time of bequest (birth within 6 months).
The hukm is sahih, not wajib.
2
Understand the distinction from hibah
Hibah requires immediate ownership (tamlik) and guardianship (wilayah) which a fetus lacks. Bequest does not require immediate ownership — it is a future transfer upon death.
This points to the core difference between immediate and deferred transfers.
3
Trace the analogy with inheritance
The author compares bequest to inheritance (miraath): both involve succession (istikhlaf) rather than immediate ownership. Since a fetus can inherit if born alive, it can also receive a bequest.
The shared illa is the lack of immediate ownership requirement.
4
Examine the a fortiori argument from fruits
The text argues that bequest of future fruits is valid, so bequest for a known fetus is even more valid (bi-darajah ula). This strengthens the qiyas.
This is an example of qiyas jaliy.
5
Apply the condition
The fetus must exist physically in the womb at the time of bequest, indicated by birth within six months. If not, the bequest is invalid.
Six months is the minimum pregnancy duration in Hanafi fiqh for a valid child.

The meaning is unlike hibah because it is pure ownership and no one has guardianship over the fetus to make it the owner of something. Nevertheless, secondly, because the fetus is at the stage of coming into existence, because the discussion is in a situation where its existence at the time of bequest is known. And due to the need of the deceased and his helplessness, the chapter of bequest is very vast. Therefore, a bequest for something non-existent is valid, like in fruits; thus a bequest for something existent is valid all the more. Explanation: If a child is in the womb and any of his ancestors dies, the fetus also receives inheritance. And inheritance and bequest are said to be two sisters. Therefore, making a bequest for the fetus is permissible. Similarly, if a bequest is made of the fetus itself, that is also permissible. For example, he says: 'The child in my slave-girl's womb should be given to such-and-such person.' This is also valid and the bequest is permissible. But this bequest for the fetus or of the fetus is permissible only when the fetus exists in the womb, the sign of which is that the child is born in less than six months from the time of the bequest. Then the author stated the proof for both separately. A bequest for the fetus is valid. Its proof is that a bequest does not make one an owner immediately, but like inheritance, it also involves succession (istikhlaf). So just as inheritance is valid, similarly bequest is valid because bequest and inheritance are parallels of each other. Except for this difference: a bequest is revoked by revocation, but inheritance is not revoked, because in bequest there is an aspect of ownership from the testator, and where there is an aspect of ownership, revocation is permissible. In inheritance there is no ownership but mere succession. A bequest for the fetus is valid, but hibah for the fetus is not valid because hibah is pure ownership without succession. And since the fetus has not yet been born, no one can have guardianship over it to make it the owner of something, so hibah is impermissible. As for the bequest of the fetus, it is also valid because our discussion is about such a fetus whose existence at the time of bequest is known. And when its existence is known, then that fetus will come into existence after a few days anyway. Considering the need and humility of the deceased, the chapter of bequest is vast. And the proof of its vastness is that if someone makes a bequest: 'The fruit that will come in my garden is bequeathed to so-and-so,' this bequest is valid even though the fruit does not yet exist. So for something that exists, the bequest is valid all the more.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The passage addresses the validity of making a bequest (wasiyyah) for a fetus (janin) in the womb. The text first distinguishes wasiyyah from hibah: hibah is pure ownership (tamlik), and no one has guardianship (wilayah) over the fetus to make it an owner. Therefore hibah to a fetus is invalid. In contrast, wasiyyah is akin to inheritance (miraath) because it involves succession (istikhlaf) rather than immediate ownership. The passage then provides a proof: since inheritance is valid for the fetus (as it inherits if born alive), and wasiyyah and inheritance are analogous, wasiyyah for the fetus is also valid. However, there is a condition: the fetus must exist in the womb at the time of bequest, indicated by birth within six months of the bequest (a classic Hanafi sign that the conception preceded the death). The passage further argues that the chapter of wasiyyah is intentionally broad due to the deceased's need; thus bequests for future fruits are also valid by analogy (qiyas). Hence a bequest for a known fetus is valid a fortiori (bi-darajah ula). The text also notes that a bequest for the fetus itself (e.g., 'my slave's child to so-and-so') is valid. The reasoning follows Hanafi legal principles, relying on qiyas and istihsan.

Key Terms
وصیت (wasiyyah): A legal directive made by a person regarding his property to take effect after his death.
حمل (haml): A fetus in the womb; a potential legal person if born alive.
ہبہ (hibah): A gift; an immediate transfer of ownership without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because bequest is obligatory and gift is optional.
D) Because the fetus is considered a legal person for all transactions.

Key Takeaway
A bequest to a fetus is valid in Hanafi law because it is a deferred transfer (succeeding the testator) analogous to inheritance, not an immediate grant.

Wasiyyah — Bequest
intermediate
8 min
Bequest of a Pregnant Slave and Exception of the Fetus
Ruling with proof and general principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequeathing a pregnant slave with exception of the fetus and explain the general principle that whatever can be independently contracted can be excepted.

Core Idea
If the fetus can be bequeathed alone, it can be excepted from the mother's bequest.

This passage from Al-Hidayah (via Al-Quduri) deals with a specific case: a testator bequeaths a pregnant slave-girl but excepts her fetus. The Hanafi school rules that both the bequest and the exception are valid. The reasoning hinges on a general legal principle derived from sales: whatever can be made the sole subject of a contract can also be excepted from a larger contract. This lesson will walk you through the ruling, its linguistic basis, and the principle it exemplifies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The testator bequeaths a pregnant slave-girl but excepts the fetus. The ruling: both the bequest and the exception are valid (sahih). The fetus does not enter the bequest.
2
Understand the linguistic point
The word 'jariyah' (slave-girl) linguistically does not include the fetus (haml). However, if the testator mentions the slave-girl absolutely without exception, the fetus is included as a follower (taba’). The exception removes this following relationship.
This is a Hanafi principle: inclusion by taba'iyyah can be negated by explicit exception.
3
Trace the proof
The proof is a general principle (qa’idah kulliyyah) established in the Book of Sales: anything that can be independently contracted can be excepted from a contract. Since a bequest of the fetus alone is valid (if the testator so intends), its exception from a bequest of the mother is valid.
The principle is qiyas from the rules of sale to bequest.
4
Consider the default without exception
If the testator simply says 'I bequeath my slave-girl' without mentioning the fetus, the fetus is automatically included as a follower. The exception is thus a way to exclude it.
5
Recognize the general principle's scope
The principle is not limited to bequest; it applies to any contract. Whatever cannot be independently contracted (e.g., a part that is inseparable) cannot be excepted.
This shows the systematic nature of Hanafi fiqh: one principle governs multiple chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حامله باندی کو دسرے کیلئے وصیت کرنا اور حمل کا استنی کرنا
قال ومن أوصى بجارية الأحملها صحت الوصية والاستثناء لان اسم الجارية لايتناول الحمل لفظا ولكنه يستحق بالاطلاق تبعا فاذا افرد الام بالوصية صح افرادها ولانه يصح افراد الحمل بالوصية فجاز استثناء ه وهذا هو الاصل ان ما يصح افراده بالعقد يصح استثناء ة منه اذلا فرق بينهما وما لا يصح افراده بالعقد لا يصح استثناء ة منه وقد مرفى البيوع
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس نے وصیت کی باندی کی مگر اس کے حمل کی تو وصیت اور استثنا صحیح ہے اس لئے کہ لفظ جار یہ لفظی اعتبار سے حمل کو شامل نہیں ہے لیکن لفظ جاریہ کے مطلق بولنے کی وجہ سے تبعا حمل کا استحقاق ثابت ہو جاتا ہے پس جب موصی نے تنہا ماں کی وصیت کی تو ماں کا افراد صحیح ہے اور اس لئے کہ حمل کی وصیت کا افراد صحیح ہے تو اس کا استثناء جائز ہے اور یہی قاعدہ کلیہ ہے کہ عقد میں جس چیز کا افراد صحیح ہے تو عقد سے اس کا استثناء صحیح ہے اس لئے کہ ان دونوں کے درمیان کوئی فرق نہیں ہے اور عقد میں جس کا افراد بیچ نہیں ہے تو عقد سے اس کا استثناء صحیح نہیں ہے اور یہ قاعدہ کلیہ کتاب البیوع میں گزر چکا ہے۔ تشریح اگر موصی نے باندی کی وصیت کی اور اس کے عمل کا استثناء کر دیا تو باندی کی وصیت صحیح ہے اور استثناء بھی صحیح ہے لہذا حمل وصیت میں داخل نہ ہوگا کیونکہ لفظی اعتبار سے لفظ جاریہ عمل کو شامل نہیں ہے لیکن اگر مطلق جاریہ بولا اور حمل کا استثنا نہیں کیا تو تابع ہونے کی وجہ سے حمل بھی باندی کے تحت داخل ہوگا اور وصیت درست ہوگی۔ تو جب موصی نے تنہا ماں کی وصیت کی تو وصیت درست ہے اور اگر تنہا حمل کی وصیت کرے تو وہ بھی جائز ہے لہذا اس کا استثنا بھی کیونکہ قاعدہ کلیہ یہی ہے کہ تنہا جس چیز کا عقد ہو سکتا ہے تو اس کا عقد سے استثناء بھی ہوسکتا ہے اور تنہا جس کا عقد نہیں ہو سکتا اس کا استثناء بھی صحیح نہیں ہو سکتا۔
Bequeathing a pregnant slave-girl to someone and excepting the fetus. He said: And whoever bequeaths a slave-girl with her pregnancy, the bequest and the exception are valid because the word 'slave-girl' does not include the fetus linguistically, but it is established by the absolute expression as a follower (taba'). So when he makes a bequest of the mother alone, its isolation is valid. And because it is valid to make a bequest of the fetus alone, so its exception is permissible. And this is the general principle: whatever it is valid to isolate in a contract, it is valid to except it from the contract, for there is no difference between them. And whatever it is not valid to isolate in a contract, it is not valid to except it from it. And this has been mentioned in the Book of Sales. Translation: Al-Quduri said: And whoever bequeaths a slave-girl but excepts her pregnancy, the bequest and the exception are valid because the word 'slave-girl' does not include the fetus linguistically, but due to the absolute expression, the entitlement of the fetus is established as a follower. So when the testator bequeaths the mother alone, its isolation is valid. And because the isolation of the bequest of the fetus is valid, so its exception is permissible. And this is the general principle: whatever it is valid to isolate in a contract, its exception from the contract is valid, because there is no difference between them. And whatever it is not valid to isolate in a contract, its exception is not valid. And this general principle has passed in the Book of Sales. Explanation: If the testator bequeaths the slave-girl and excepts her fetus, then the bequest of the slave-girl is valid and the exception is also valid. Therefore, the fetus will not enter the bequest because linguistically the word 'slave-girl' does not include the fetus. But if he says the absolute 'slave-girl' and does not except the fetus, then as a follower the fetus enters under the slave-girl and the bequest is valid. So when the testator bequeaths the mother alone, the bequest is valid, and if he bequeaths the fetus alone, that is also permissible. Hence its exception is also [valid] because the general principle is that whatever can be contracted alone, its exception from a contract is also possible, and whatever cannot be contracted alone, its exception is not valid.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof and a legal principle. The passage deals with a case where a testator bequeaths a pregnant slave-girl (jariyah) but excludes (yastathni) the fetus she carries. The ruling is that both the bequest of the slave-girl and the exception of the fetus are valid (sahih). The reasoning: The word 'jariyah' linguistically does not include the fetus (haml). However, if the testator mentions the slave-girl absolutely without exception, the fetus is included by way of being a follower (taba'). Thus the testator can choose to exclude it. The proof: It is valid to make a bequest of the fetus alone (if the fetus is a potential recipient), so it is valid to except it from a bequest of the mother. This is based on a general principle (qaidah kulliyyah) established in the Book of Sales (Kitab al-Buyu'): Whatever can be independently contracted (afrad bi al-'aqd) can be excepted from a contract, and whatever cannot be independently contracted cannot be excepted. The passage then confirms that if only the mother is bequeathed, the fetus follows, but if an exception is made, it does not. The commentary also clarifies that if the fetus alone is bequeathed, that is also valid, so its exception is consistent. This illustrates the Hanafi methodology of deriving rulings from general principles.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): An exception; excluding something from a statement or contract.
افراد (ifrad): Isolation; treating something as an independent subject of a contract.
تبع (taba'): Following; inclusion of something as a subordinate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the exception of the fetus from the bequest of a pregnant slave-girl valid in Hanafi law?
A) Because the word 'slave-girl' linguistically includes the fetus, so exception removes it.
B) Because the fetus can be bequeathed independently, so it can be excepted.
C) Because the fetus has no legal status, so exception is automatic.
D) Because the exception is a form of revocation.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an exception in a bequest depends on whether the excepted item can be independently contracted: a principle that unifies sales and bequest law.

Bequest of a Slave-Girl and Exception of Her Child
Ruling with proof and general maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequeathing a slave-girl while excluding her unborn child and apply the general rule that whatever can be contracted alone can be excepted from a contract.

Core Idea
A bequest of a slave-girl excludes her child only if explicitly excepted; both the bequest and exception are valid.

This passage from Quduri presents a key ruling on bequest (wasiyyah) of a slave-girl and the exception of her unborn child. The author not only states the ruling but also provides a linguistic justification and a general legal principle. This lesson will walk you through the ruling, its proof, and the broader qa'idah that governs exceptions in contracts and bequests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the explicit ruling from Quduri: If someone bequeaths a slave-girl and excepts her child, both the bequest and exception are valid. Determine the hukm level (sahih).
The ruling is a straightforward statement from the matn.
2
Analyze the linguistic proof
Understand why the word 'jariyah' (slave-girl) does not include the child (haml) literally. The proof is linguistic: the term refers to the mother alone. However, as a general rule, when the mother is bequeathed without exception, the child is included as a follower (tab').
The distinction between literal meaning and implicit inclusion is crucial.
3
Extract the general rule
Identify the qa'idah: 'Whatever can be included alone in a contract, its exception from the contract is valid; and whatever cannot be included alone, its exception is not valid.' Write the Arabic and understand its scope.
This rule applies to all contracts, not just bequests.
4
Apply the qa'idah to the specific case
Since a child (unborn slave) can be bequeathed alone, its exception from the bequest of the mother is valid. Conversely, if something cannot be bequeathed alone (e.g., a non-property), its exception would be invalid.
The rule provides a consistent criterion.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dimension
Some schools may hold that the child cannot be excluded because it is part of the mother. Understand the basis of disagreement and the Hanafi tarjih.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Translation: Quduri said: And whoever makes a bequest of a slave-girl except her child, then the bequest and the exception are valid, because the word 'jariyah' does not include the child literally, but by uttering 'jariyah' absolutely, the child's right is established indirectly. So when the testator bequeathed only the mother, the exclusion of the mother is valid, and because making a bequest of the child alone is valid, therefore excepting it is permissible. And this is a general rule: whatever can be included alone in a contract, its exception from the contract is valid, because there is no difference between the two; and whatever cannot be included alone in a contract, its exception from the contract is not valid. This general rule has passed in the Book of Sales. Commentary: If the testator bequeathed a slave-girl and excluded her child, the bequest of the slave-girl is valid and the exception is also valid, so the child does not enter the bequest because the word 'jariyah' does not include the child literally. But if he uttered 'jariyah' absolutely and did not exclude the child, then due to its being a follower, the child also enters under the slave-girl and the bequest is valid. So when the testator bequeathed only the mother, the bequest is valid, and if he bequeathed only the child, that is also permissible, therefore its exception is also [valid] because the general rule is this: whatever can be contracted alone, its exception from the contract is possible, and whatever cannot be contracted alone, its exception is not valid.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage begins with a ruling from Quduri on bequest of a slave-girl and exception of her child. The ruling: if someone bequeaths a slave-girl but excludes her child, both the bequest and the exception are valid. The reason is linguistic: the word 'jariyah' (slave-girl) does not literally include the child (haml). However, if the testator simply says 'my slave-girl' without exception, the child is included indirectly as a dependent (tab'). The testator then provides a general qa'idah (rule): whatever can be the subject of a contract in isolation, its exception from the contract is valid; and whatever cannot be contracted in isolation, its exception is not valid. For example, the child alone can be bequeathed, so the exception is valid. This qa'idah is referenced to the Book of Sales. The commentary (Tashrih) clarifies the application: if the testator explicitly excludes the child, the bequest of the mother stands without the child; if he does not exclude, the child follows the mother in the bequest. The rule is also applied to the reverse: bequeathing only the child is valid, so excluding the child from a bequest of the mother is also valid.

Key Terms
وصیت (wasiyyah): A bequest or will; a legal disposition of property to take effect after death.
باندی (bandi): A slave-girl (jariyah).
حمل (haml): The unborn child in the womb (pregnancy).
استثناء (istithna): Exception, exclusion of something from a general statement or contract.
قاعدہ کلیہ (qa'idah kulliyyah): A general legal rule that applies to multiple cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The child is included as a follower (tab').
C) The bequest is invalid because the child must be explicitly mentioned.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates both the bequest of a slave-girl and the exception of her unborn child, based on the linguistic scope of 'jariyah' and the general qa'idah that independent bequeathability permits exception.

Revocation of Will Is Permissible
Ruling on the permissibility of revoking a will, with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revocation of a will and explain the two proofs (qiyas to hibah and bay').

Core Idea
A will can be revoked anytime before death because it is an incomplete gift.

The Quduri text states clearly that a testator may revoke his will. This lesson unpacks the two analogical proofs that establish this permissibility, drawing from the logic of gifts and sales. Understanding these proofs reveals why a will is not binding until the testator's death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? What is the hukm (level of permissibility)? Find the explicit statement in the source.
The ruling is ja'iz (permissible) for the testator to revoke his will.
2
Locate the first proof (qiyas to hibah)
The author analogizes a will to a gift (hibah) before delivery. Both are gratuitous transfers (tabarru') that are not yet complete. Identify the 'illa: incompleteness of the tabarru'.
Gifts can be revoked before delivery; similarly, wills can be revoked before death.
3
Trace the second proof (qiyas to bay')
The second analogy compares the will to a sale contract where the offer (ijab) can be revoked before acceptance (qabul). For a will, acceptance occurs only after death, so the offer can be revoked earlier.
The principle: ijab is revocable before qabul.
4
Summarize the underlying principle
Both analogies point to a common principle: a legal disposition that is not yet binding and lacks acceptance can be withdrawn by the party who made it.
This principle applies to all non-binding gratuitous acts (tabarru' ghayr lazim).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ويجوز للموصى الرجوع عن الوصية لانه تبرع لم يتم فجاز الرجوع عنه كالهبة وقد حققناه في كتاب الهبة ولان الــقـبــول يتوقف عـلـى الـمـوت والايـجـاب يـصـح ابـطـالـه قبـل الـقـبـول كـمـا فـي البيع ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور موصی کے لئے وصیت سے رجوع جائز ہے اس لئے کہ وصیت ایک تبرع سے جو نام نہیں ہوا تو ہبہ کے مثل اس سے رجوع جائز ہے اور ہم اس کو کتاب الہبہ میں بیان کر چکے ہیں اور اس لئے کہ قبول موت پر موقوف ہے اور قبول سے پہلے ایجاب کا ابطال صیح سے جیسے بیع میں ۔ تشریح اگر موصی وصیت سے رجوع کرے تو جائز ہے کیونکہ وصیت تبرع ہے اور تبرع جب تک تام نہ ہو تو اس سے رجوع جائز ہے جیسے ہبہ کی تمامیت سے پہلے اس سے رجوع جائز ہے جس کا تفصیلی بیان کتاب الہبہ میں گزر چکا ہے اور اس لئے کہ قبول وہ معتبر ہے جو موصی کی موت کے بعد ہو ( کمائر ) اور اصول یہ ہے کہ اگر ایجاب کے بعد قبول نہ آیا ہو تو قبول سے پہلے پہلے ایجاب کا ابطال جائز ہے جیسے بیع کے اندر بھی یہی حکم ہے۔
He said: It is permissible for the testator to revoke the will, because it is a gratuitous transfer (tabarru') that has not been completed, so revocation is permitted, like revocation of a gift (hibah). And we have verified this in the Book of Gifts. And because acceptance (qabul) is contingent upon death, and the offer (ijab) can be revoked before acceptance, as in the case of sale (bay'). Translation: Quduri said: It is permissible for the testator to revoke the will, because the will is a gratuitous transfer that has not become complete, so revocation is permissible like a gift, and we have explained this in the Book of Gifts. And because acceptance is dependent on death, and before acceptance, the offer can be revoked, as in sale. Commentary: If the testator revokes the will, it is permissible because the will is a gratuitous transfer (tabarru'), and as long as a gratuitous transfer is not complete, revocation is permissible, just as revocation of a gift before its completion is permissible as detailed in the Book of Gifts. And because the valid acceptance is that which occurs after the testator's death, and the principle is that if acceptance has not come after the offer, then the offer can be revoked before acceptance, as this same ruling applies in sale.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that a testator may revoke his will. The proof is twofold: analogy to hibah (gift) and analogy to bay' (sale). First, a will is a tabarru' (gratuitous transfer) that is not completed until the testator's death, so just as a gift can be revoked before delivery, a will can be revoked before death. Second, in a sale, the offer (ijab) can be withdrawn before acceptance (qabul); similarly, the will's acceptance only occurs after death, so the testator can revoke the offer before that. The commentary (Tashrih) emphasizes that the will is incomplete until death, and the principle of revocation before acceptance applies.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation; withdrawing a legal disposition before it becomes binding.
تبرع (tabarru'): A gratuitous transfer, a voluntary gift without consideration.
ایجاب (ijab): The offer, the initial declaration of a legal transaction.
قبول (qabul): Acceptance; the second declaration in a contract or the consent of the legatee after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is revocation of a will permissible?
A) Because the testator owns the property until death.
B) Because the will is a gratuitous transfer not yet completed, like a gift.
C) Because the Quduri text says so without reason.
D) Because the testator can change his mind anytime.

Key Takeaway
A will is revocable at any time before death because it is an incomplete gratuitous act.

Explicit and Implicit Revocation of a Will
Ruling that revocation occurs by explicit statement or by acts indicating revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two methods of revoking a will and explain how implicit conduct (dalalah) is considered equivalent to explicit revocation, using the analogy of khiyar in sale.

Core Idea
Implicit conduct revokes a will just as explicit words do — intent proven by action.

In the previous section, we learned that a testator may revoke a will at any time. This lesson details the two methods of revocation: explicit statement (sarih) and implicit conduct (dalalah). The Hanafi position is that both are equally effective. The key proof is an analogy to a sale with an option condition (khiyar al-shart), where conduct indicating abandonment invalidates the option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is revocation of a will by the testator. The ruling is that it is valid if done explicitly or implicitly.
2
Distinguish the two methods
Explicit revocation uses clear words like 'I revoke my will'. Implicit revocation is conduct that clearly indicates the intention, such as selling the bequeathed property.
The passage states that dalalah takes the place of the phrase 'I have revoked'.
3
Examine the proof: analogy to khiyar
The Hanafi proof is qiyas: just as in a sale with an option, the buyer's conduct indicating abandonment invalidates the option, similarly, the testator's conduct indicating revocation invalidates the will.
The common 'illa is that the act clearly shows the intent to relinquish the right.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that a testator need not utter formal words; any clear act suffices. This provides flexibility and prevents disputes over missing formalities.

He said: And if he explicitly revokes, or does something that indicates revocation, then it is considered revocation. The explicit is clear, and similarly the implicit (dalalah), because the implicit acts like the explicit, so it takes the place of his saying 'I have revoked', and it becomes like a sale with a condition of option (khiyar), where the option is invalidated by implication. Translation: Quduri said: When the testator explicitly revokes, or does an act that indicates revocation, then in both cases revocation occurs. The explicit is clear, and similarly the implicit, because the implicit acts like the explicit, so it takes the place of the phrase 'I have revoked', and it becomes like a sale with a condition of option, where the option is invalidated by implication. Commentary: In the previous section, it was learned that revocation of a will is permissible for the testator. Then he says that there are two methods of revocation: (1) explicit revocation, (2) doing an act that indicates revocation. In both cases, revocation is valid, because the implicit is at the level of explicit revocation, and the implicit takes the place of 'I have revoked'. For example, if a buyer has stipulated an option (khiyar sharat), then doing an act that indicates cancellation of the option invalidates the option.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage details the two methods of revocation: explicit statement (sarih) and implied conduct (dalalah). The explicit is straightforward (e.g., 'I revoke my will'). The implied is an act that clearly shows intention to revoke, such as selling the bequeathed property. The commentator draws an analogy to a sale with an option (khiyar): if the buyer does something that indicates abandonment of the option, the option is invalidated, even without explicit words. The key principle is that implicit conduct (dalalah) has the same legal effect as explicit statement in indicating revocation.

Key Terms
صریح (sarih): Explicit, clear statement, unambiguous.
دلالت (dalalah): Implication, circumstantial evidence, conduct that indicates an intention.
خیار (khiyar): Option, a contractual right to cancel or confirm a transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It requires a court order to be effective.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, revocation of a will is valid by either explicit words or by any act that clearly indicates the intention to revoke, based on analogy to the invalidation of an option (khiyar) by conduct.

Transformative Acts as Implicit Revocation of a Will
Ruling on implicit revocation through acts severing ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that any act which severs the owner's right on another's property constitutes an implicit revocation of a will when done by the testator, and identify an example from the Book of Ghasp.

Core Idea
If grinding wheat severs the usurper's right, it also revokes a will.

In the previous lesson we learned that selling the bequeathed property or changing its description can revoke a will. The author now adds a third method: any act that, if done on someone else's property, would sever the owner's right, also counts as revocation when the testator does it on the bequeathed property. This principle is illustrated with an example from the Book of Ghasp, where grinding usurped wheat transfers ownership and turns the original right into a claim for compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
Look for any act done by the testator on the bequeathed property after making the will. The act must be something that, if done on another's property, would terminate the owner's right (e.g., grinding wheat into flour, building on land, changing the substance).
The act must be transformative — it changes the property's identity or nature, not just its location.
2
Check the effect on owner's right
Ask: Does this act sever the original owner's right to the property? In the example, grinding wheat flour attaches the usurper's right to the flour, and the original owner's right becomes a right to compensation. If yes, the testator's act is evidence of revocation.
The principle is borrowed from the Book of Ghasp: if the act would cut off the owner's right in a usurpation case, it also cuts off the legatee's right under the will.
3
Conclude that the will is revoked
Once the act is identified and established to sever the owner's right, the ruling is that the testator has implicitly revoked the will. The legatee no longer has a claim to the bequeathed property; instead they may have a right to compensation (as in ghasp) or nothing.
This is a default ruling — no need for explicit words of revocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then every act that if a person does in the property of another, the owner's right is severed by it; so when the testator does it, it is considered revocation. And we have enumerated these acts in the Book of Usurpation (Ghasp). Translation: Then every act that a person does in the property of another that severs the owner's right; when the testator does it, it becomes a revocation. And we have explained these acts in the Book of Ghasp. Commentary: Here the author lays down some principles. This is the first principle: he says that if someone does an act in another's property by which the owner's right is severed – for example, if wheat was usurped and the usurper ground it into flour, then the usurper's right becomes attached to the flour, and the owner of the usurped property will be compensated for it. The author states that if the testator does such an act in the bequeathed property after the will, then this act is evidence of revocation. The detailed explanation of these acts has passed in the Book of Ghasp.

Commentary
This passage introduces a third method of implicit revocation: any act that, if done by someone else in the property of another, would sever the owner's right, also constitutes revocation when done by the testator on the bequeathed property. The example given is from the Book of Ghasp: if a usurper grinds wheat into flour, the owner's right to the wheat is severed and replaced by a right to compensation. Similarly, if the testator does such a transformative act to the bequeathed property, it indicates revocation. The details of such acts are in the book on usurpation.

Key Terms

حق مالک (haqq al-malik): The right of the owner to his property.

Inseparable Addition as Implicit Revocation of Will
Ruling on acts causing inseparable addition to bequeathed property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acts that constitute implicit revocation of a will and those that do not, based on the Hanafi criterion of inseparable addition.

Core Idea
An inseparable addition to bequeathed property that prevents delivery without it constitutes implicit revocation; changes to accessories do not.

In Hanafi fiqh, a testator may implicitly revoke a will by actions inconsistent with its execution. This lesson examines one class of such actions: those that add something to the bequeathed property that cannot be separated without destroying the property. We will analyze the legal criterion and apply it to concrete examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its effect
Read the examples in the passage: mixing sawiq with ghee, building a structure in the house, stuffing a cushion, adding a lining or outer cover. What do these have in common? They all add a substance that becomes part of the property.
The addition is physical and inseparable.
2
Assess delivery feasibility
Ask: After the testator's act, can the original bequeathed item be delivered to the legatee without also delivering the addition? If not, the act indicates an intention to revoke.
The key is that delivery is impossible without the addition.
3
Distinguish accessory changes
Contrast with plastering the house or demolishing a wall. These are changes to an accessory (tabi') that do not affect the principal property's identity. Such acts do not constitute revocation.
Principal vs. accessory distinction is critical.
4
Apply the rule
Given a new scenario, first check if the act creates an inseparable addition. If yes, and if delivery without that addition is impossible, then the bequest is revoked. Otherwise, it remains valid.
The ruling is based on the impossibility of delivery due to the change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كل فعل يوجب زيادة فى الموصى به ولا يمكن تسليم العين الابها فهو رجوع اذا فعله مثل السويق يلته بالسمن والداريبنى فيها الموصى والقطن يحشوبه والبطانة يبطن بها و الظهارة يظهر بها لانه لا يمكنه تسليمه بدون الزيادة ولا يمكن نقضها لانه حصل فى ملك الوصى من جهته بخلاف تجصيص الدار الموصى بها وهدم بنائها لانه تصرف في التابع ترجمہ اور ہر ایسا فعل جو موصی یہ میں زیادتی کو ثابت کرے اور عین کی تسلیم اس زیادتی کے بغیر ممکن نہ ہو تو جب موصی نے یہ کام انجام دیا ہوتو یہ رجوع ہے جیسے ستو جس کو گھی میں ملا دیا ہو اور گھر جس میں موجی نے عمارت بنادی ہو اور روئی جس کو موصی نے بھر لیا ہو اور استر کا کپڑا جس کا موصی نے استر بنالیا ہو اور ابر جس کا موجی نے ابرا بنا لیا ہو۔ اس لئے کہ اس زیادتی کے بغیر موجی کو اس کی تسلیم ممکن نہیں
And every act that necessitates an increase in the bequeathed property, and delivery of the property (ayn) is not possible without it, then when the testator does it, it is considered revocation: such as mixing barley porridge (sawiq) with ghee, or building a structure in the bequeathed house, or stuffing cotton (filling a cushion), or making a lining (batinah) or an outer cover (zaharah). Because he cannot deliver it without the increase, and the increase cannot be undone since it happened in the testator's property by his own action, unlike plastering the bequeathed house or demolishing its building, because that is a change in the accessory (tabi'). Translation: And every act that causes an addition in the bequeathed property, and delivery of the property is not possible without that addition, so when the testator does it, it is revocation: like sawiq mixed with ghee, a house in which the testator built a structure, cotton that he stuffed, a lining that he made, and an outer covering that he made. Because he cannot deliver it without the addition, and the addition cannot be undone because it occurred in the testator's property by his own act, unlike plastering the bequeathed house or demolishing its structure, because that is an act on the accessory.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents another category of implicit revocation: acts that add something inseparable to the bequeathed property, making it impossible to deliver the original item without the addition. Examples: mixing sawiq (barley porridge) with ghee so it cannot be separated; building a structure in the bequeathed house; stuffing cotton into a cushion; adding a lining or outer cover to a garment. The key is that the addition becomes part of the property and cannot be removed without damaging it, so the testator's act indicates an intention to revoke the bequest. Contrast with plastering a house or demolishing its building: these are changes to the accessory (tabi') and do not constitute revocation. The ruling is based on the fact that the testator cannot deliver the bequeathed item in its original state, so the bequest is effectively revoked.

Key Terms
موصی یہ (musa fi): The bequeathed property; the subject of the will.
زیادہ (ziyadah): Addition, increase that becomes inseparable from the property.
تابع (tabi'): Accessory, something that follows the principal property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

An act that adds something inseparable to bequeathed property, making delivery of the original impossible, constitutes implicit revocation of the will.

Two Methods of Revoking a Will
Introduces two methods of revocation from a will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit (ṣarāḥat) and implicit (dalālat) revocation of a will and state the Hanafi ruling that both are valid.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a will is validly revoked either by explicit words or by any action that clearly indicates the testator's intention to revoke.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains that revocation of a will (al-rujū‘ ‘an al-waṣiyyah) is not limited to verbal statements. The author establishes that an action that clearly indicates the testator’s intention to revoke — such as destroying the written document, selling the bequeathed property, or any other act inconsistent with the will — carries the same legal weight as an explicit declaration. The ruling is supported by an analogy to a sale with a stipulated option, where certain actions by the buyer void the option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is revocation of a will (al-rujū‘ ‘an al-waṣiyyah). The Hanafi ruling is that revocation is valid (sahih) and can occur in two ways.
2
Recognize the two methods
The text explicitly names two methods: ṣarāḥat (explicit verbal statement) and dalālat (action that indicates revocation). Understand each term.
Ṣarāḥat is clear, unambiguous speech. Dalālat is a physical act that clearly shows intent to revoke.
3
Understand the legal equivalence
The author states that dalālat is in the same rank (darajah) as ṣarāḥat. This means the legal effect is identical: both methods revoke the will.
The text says: 'indication is in the same rank as explicit revocation' and 'stands in the place of his statement "I have annulled it"'.
4
Study the analogy to sale with option
The author draws an analogy to a sale with a stipulated option (khayār al-sharṭ). In such a sale, if the buyer performs an action that indicates annulment of the option, the option is voided. Similarly, a testator's action can void the will.
This analogy reinforces that actions can have the same legal effect as explicit words when they clearly indicate intention.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi school accepts both methods, but some other schools require explicit verbal revocation only. Recognize that this is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
Know the Hanafi position (muftā bihi) for fatwa purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Explicit is obvious, and similarly [implicit] indication, because indication acts like the explicit, so it stands in the place of his statement 'I have annulled it', and it becomes like a sale with the option condition where the option becomes void due to indication. Commentary
This passage establishes the basic principle of revocation of a will. The author explains that there are two ways a testator can revoke his will: explicitly by saying so, or implicitly by performing an action that clearly indicates the intention to revoke. The implicit action is considered legally equivalent to an explicit statement, just as in a sale with a stipulated option, certain actions by the buyer can void the option. The commentary clarifies that both methods are valid because the indication carries the same legal weight as the explicit declaration.

Key Terms

دلالت (Dalālat): An action that indicates the testator's intention to revoke the will.

Revocation by Actions Cutting Off the Owner's Right
States rule: actions that cut off owner's right in another's property are revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that certain actions constitute revocation of a will and apply it to identify whether a given action severs the testator's right.

Core Idea
An action that transforms a bequeathed item's identity revokes the will.

In Hanafi law, revocation of a will can occur not only by explicit words but also by actions that fundamentally alter the bequeathed property. This lesson draws an analogy from the chapter on usurpation (ghaṣb) to define such actions. Understanding this principle helps you see how the school extends rulings from one area of fiqh to another through qiyās.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action category
Read the passage and note that it refers to 'every action that, if done in another's property, cuts off the owner's right.' Highlight the key phrase: 'ينقطع به حق المالك' (cuts off the right of the owner).
The action must be such that the owner cannot reclaim the property in its original form.
2
Understand the analogy from ghaṣb
The passage explicitly refers to actions enumerated in the Book of Usurpation. The example given in the commentary is grinding wheat into flour. In ghaṣb, such an action vests rights in the usurper and obligates compensation to the owner. The testator's similar action on the bequeathed item is analogized to this.
The common link (illa) is the severance of the owner's right by changing the item's essence.
3
Apply the rule to new cases
Test other actions: e.g., mixing bequeathed oil with something else, or building with bequeathed bricks. Only those that make the item unrecognizable or inseparable from another property qualify.
Simple relocation or cleaning does not sever the owner's right.
4
Evaluate the proof
The proof is qiyās from ghaṣb. Consider its strength: the ruling in ghaṣb is established by texts and consensus; the analogy is jali (clear) because the illa (severance of right) is manifest. However, some schools may object that revocation requires explicit statement, hence the Hanafi stance is not universally accepted.
This illustrates how Hanafi usul employs qiyās jaliy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كل فعل لو فعله الانسان فى ملك الغير ينقطع به حق المالك فاذا فعله الموصى كان رجوعا وقد عددنا هذه الافاعيل في كتاب الغصب
ترجمہ پھر ہروہ کام جس کو انسان غیر کی ملک میں کرے جس سے مالک کا حق منقطع ہو جائے پس جب اس کام کو موصی نے کر دیا تو یہ رجوع بن جائیگا اور ان افعال کو ہم نے کتاب الغصب میں بیان کر دیا ہے۔
تشریح یہاں سے مصنف چند قواعد واصول پیش فرما رہے ہیں یہ پہلا اصول ہے فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے دوسرے کی ملکیت میں ایسا کام کر دیا جس سے مالک کا حق منقطع ہو جائے جیسے مثلاً گیہوں تھے غاصب نے ان کو پیس دیا تو اب آٹے کے ساتھ غاصب کا حق متعلق ہو گیا اور مغصوب منہ کو اس کا ضمان ادا کیا جائے گا تو مصنف نے بتایا کہ اگر موصی نے بعد وصیت موضی یہ میں ایسا تصرف کر دیا تو یہ تصرف رجوع کی دلیل ہوگا اور کتاب الغصب میں ان افعال کا تفصیلی بیان گزر چکا ہے۔
Then, every action that, if a person does it in the property of another, cuts off the right of the owner; if the testator does it, it becomes a revocation. And we have enumerated these actions in the Book of Usurpation. Translation: Then, every action that a person does in the property of another that cuts off the owner's right; when the testator does it, it becomes a revocation, and we have explained these actions in the Book of Usurpation. Explanation: From here, the author presents several rules and principles. This is the first principle. He says: If someone does an action in another's property that cuts off the owner's right, like, for example, wheat that a usurper has ground, so now the usurper's right becomes attached to the flour, and the owner of the usurped item will be compensated for it. So the author stated that if the testator makes such a disposition in the bequeathed item after the will, this disposition will be evidence of revocation, and the detailed explanation of these actions has passed in the Book of Usurpation.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a key principle: any action that, if done by a usurper in another's property, would sever the owner's right (such as grinding wheat into flour, where the usurper becomes entitled to the flour and must compensate the owner), if done by the testator to the bequeathed item, counts as a revocation of the will. The commentary explains that this is the first of several rules, and the types of such actions are detailed in the chapter on usurpation (ghaṣb). The legal reasoning is based on qiyās (analogy): the action destroys the specific identity of the item, making it impossible to return it in its original form, thus indicating the testator's intention to revoke the bequest.

Key Terms
غصب (Ghaṣb): Usurpation: taking another's property wrongfully.
موصىٰ به (Mūṣā bihi): The item bequeathed in the will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions by a testator on a bequeathed item would count as a revocation according to this rule?
A) Washing the item
B) Grinding wheat into flour
C) Plastering a wall
D) Demolishing a structure

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, any action that fundamentally alters the bequeathed item, making it impossible to return in original form, automatically revokes the will.

Revocation by Inseparable Addition to Bequeathed Item
Rules on inseparable additions as revocation of a will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inseparable additions in wills and identify which actions constitute revocation.

Core Idea
Adding something inseparable to a bequeathed item revokes the will if delivery is impossible without it.

This passage explains when a testator's action on a bequeathed item is considered a revocation of the will. The Hanafi school holds that if the testator adds something that cannot be separated without damage, and the original item cannot be delivered without the addition, that action is a revocation. However, minor subordinate changes like plastering do not count.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine whether the testator's action is an inseparable addition to the bequeathed item. If yes, the action is considered a revocation of the will.
The ruling is based on the impossibility of delivering the original item without the addition.
2
Locate the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that the addition makes delivery impossible without it, analogous to grinding grain which changes the substance.
This is a qiyas reasoning: the addition alters the item such that it cannot be returned to its original state.
3
Distinguish subordinate actions
Actions like plastering or demolishing a wall affect only the subordinate parts; the main item remains deliverable. These are not revocation.
Subordinate actions do not alter the essence of the bequeathed item.
4
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Use the conditions (inseparable addition, impossibility of delivery, testator's action) to classify any given action as revocation or not.
The key is whether the addition can be removed without damaging the original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كل فعل يوجب زيادة فى الموصى به ولا يمكن تسليم العين الابها فهو رجوع اذا فعله مثل السويق يلته بالسمن والداريبنى فيها الموصى والقطن يحشوبه والبطانة يبطن بها و الظهارة يظهر بها لانه لا يمكنه تسليمه بدون الزيادة ولا يمكن نقضها لانه حصل فى ملك الوصى من جهته بخلاف تجصيص الدار الموصى بها وهدم بنائها لانه تصرف في التابع
ترجمہ اور ہر ایسا فعل جو موصی یہ میں زیادتی کو ثابت کرے اور عین کی تسلیم اس زیادتی کے بغیر ممکن نہ ہو تو جب موصی نے یہ کام انجام دیا ہوتو یہ رجوع ہے جیسے ستو جس کو گھی میں ملا دیا ہو اور گھر جس میں موجی نے عمارت بنادی ہو اور روئی جس کو موصی نے بھر لیا ہو اور استر کا کپڑا جس کا موصی نے استر بنالیا ہو اور ابر جس کا موجی نے ابرا بنا لیا ہو۔ اس لئے کہ اس زیادتی کے بغیر موجی کو اس کی تسلیم ممکن نہیں

ہے۔ اور زیادتی کا توڑ ناممکن نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ زیادتی موصی کی جانب سے موصی کی ملکیت میں حاصل ہوئی ہے بخلاف وصیت کئے ہوئے گھر چونا کرنے کے اور اس کی عمارت کو گرانے کے اس لئے کہ یہ تابع میں تصرف ہے۔
تشریح اور اگر موصی نے موضی یہ میں کچھ اضافہ کر دیا ہو کہ وہ زیادتی موضی یہ سے الگ نہ کی جاسکے اور اگر کی جائے تو اس میں نقصان ہو تو ایسا کام کرنا بھی اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ موصی نے رجوع کر لیا ہے جس کی امثلہ مصنف نے بہت سی بیان کی ہیں جو کتاب میں مذکور ہیں البتہ اگر موصی نے اس گھر میں جس کی اس نے وصیت کی ہے چونا کرادیا ہو یا ہو یا اس کو گرادیا ہو تو یہ دونوں فعل رجوع کی دلیل نہیں ہیں، اسلئے کہ اصل موجود ہے اور یہ چیزیں تابع ہیں اور موصی کا تصرف اصل میں نہیں بلکہ تابع کے اندر ہے۔
And every action that necessitates an increase in the bequeathed item, and delivery of the item is not possible without it, then if the testator does it, it is a revocation, such as barley mixed with ghee, a house in which the testator has built a structure, cotton that he has stuffed, a lining that he has lined, and an outer garment that he has made the outer covering, because he cannot deliver it without the increase, and it cannot be undone because it has occurred in the property of the testator from his side, unlike plastering the bequeathed house and demolishing its structure, because that is a disposition in the subordinate. Commentary: If the testator makes an addition to the bequeathed item that cannot be separated without damage, this is evidence of revocation. Plastering or demolishing the house is not revocation because the original remains and the action is on the subordinate.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of revocatory action: adding something inseparably to the bequeathed item so that delivering it without the addition is impossible, and the addition cannot be removed without damaging the item. Examples include mixing ground barley (satū) with ghee, building a structure in a house, stuffing cotton into a garment, or lining a garment. These actions make the bequest impossible to fulfill in its original form, so the testator's action indicates revocation. However, actions like plastering or demolishing the house are not revocation because they affect only the subordinate parts and the main structure remains intact.

Key Terms
زيادة (Ziyāda): An addition to the bequeathed item that cannot be separated without harming the original.
تابع (Tābiʿ): Subordinate part; actions on subordinate do not indicate revocation if the main object remains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions by the testator on a bequeathed house would be considered revocation?
A) Plastering the walls
B) Building an additional room
C) Demolishing a small wall
D) Painting the door

Key Takeaway
An inseparable addition by the testator to the bequeathed item that prevents delivery without it constitutes a valid revocation; subordinate changes do not.
